كما تتقوم إذا يبيع الوصي أو الأب مال الصغير من نفسه متجانساً دفعاً للضرر ، فلايصح بيعه، وهذا مثال للقسم الرابع.
Heavenly impediments. And the bequest to that [heir] is not permitted except that the Law permitted it from one-third as a consideration for him, and since the Law took charge of the bequest [by saying] 'but' i.e., from one-third of his wealth to the heirs, and invalidated his bequest to them, it becomes void in form and meaning, i.e., abrogated, i.e., the sick person [said] 'visit me', so I said: And the bequest to that: i.e., he does not have the power to bequeath for connection and fulfillment of rights, because the cause of interdiction—illness—considering the right of the heir and creditor exists, so how can the sick person own those matters? As a consideration for him, because man due to length of hope is deficient in action and says: I will do this later, so when death overtakes him he needs to make up for what he neglected in action, and in that state he is unable to perform physical acts of worship, so a concession was given for him to give charity from one-third of his wealth to compensate for some of his deficiency. And al-Tirmidhi narrated from Sa'd that he said: 'I fell ill in the year of conquest with an illness from which I almost died, so the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) visited me. I said: O Messenger of Allah, I have much wealth and only a daughter inherits from me, so should I bequeath all my wealth? He said: No. I said: Two-thirds? He said: No. I said: Half? He said: No. I said: One-third? He said: One-third, and one-third is much.' [Tirmidhi no. 2116] And since it might be thought that when the Law permitted bequest from one-third as a consideration for him, one knows that one-third is his exclusive right not related to anyone else's right so that his disposition in it is permissible, whether he bequeaths to an heir or to others, he removed that [misunderstanding] by his saying: 'Since the Law took charge...' etc. The Law etc.: by itself and did not delegate it to the sick person where He said: 'Allah instructs you concerning your children' (4:11). 'For them': i.e., for the heirs. And bequest at the beginning of Islam was obligatory by His statement: 'Prescribed for you, when death approaches one of you' (2:180), then it was abrogated by His statement 'Allah instructs you'. So bequest was obligatory at the beginning of Islam when the right of heirs was not established in Allah's Book. And since the servant is incapable of determining the amount to be bequeathed to each one, and harm used to occur to some, He indicated by His statement 'without harm' and 'you do not know which of them is nearer to you in benefit' (4:11), He revealed the verse of inheritance, i.e., 'Allah instructs you' verse, and took charge of bequest Himself. 'That becomes void': i.e., the sick person's bequest to the heir. And al-Tirmidhi and others narrated from Abu Umamah al-Bahili that he said: I heard the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) say in his sermon during the Farewell Pilgrimage: 'Indeed Allah the Blessed and Exalted has given every rightful person his right, so no bequest to an heir.' [Tirmidhi no. 2120] He said: this is a hasan hadith. 'Form': such that the sick person sells a specific asset from the estate to the heir for an equivalent value, so this is his bequest in the form of an asset where he benefits the heir with a specific asset from his property without meaning to retrieve the consideration, so it is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa in all cases, whether for equivalent value or not, because the heir's right is related to the specific asset as well as to the financial value. According to the two, it is valid for equivalent value because the right of the heirs is not invalidated regarding anything related to their right. 'Meaning': such that he acknowledges (iqrar) for one of the heirs, for it is a bequest in meaning where he acknowledges delivery of the acknowledged item to the donee without compensation, and this is not valid according to us contrary to al-Shafi'i, because in his acknowledgment to some heirs there is suspicion of lying, as it is possible that his aim is to deliver wealth to the heir without compensation, and the suspicion of the forbidden is forbidden, so it is forbidden. So this is a bequest in meaning and an acknowledgment in form. 'Reality and doubt' so that his sale to the heir is not valid at all according to Abu Hanifa, and his acknowledgment to him—i.e., to the heir—becomes void even if obtained through collection of a healthy debt, and quality is evaluated regarding them as it is evaluated regarding minors. His acknowledgment: 'Reality' such that he bequeaths to one of the heirs, and 'doubt' such that he sells good quality goods from ribawi assets for bad quality ones. Absolutely, whether for equivalent value or not. According to Abu Hanifa: this is an example of the first category, which is bequest in form. 'Healthy debt': that which was owed to him from the heir, such that the sick person acknowledges that I collected from so-and-so the heir my debt that was upon him in a state of health, because in his acknowledgment of collection there is suspicion that he wants to drop the debt from his liability, as if he gives him ownership of this amount of wealth. This is an example of the second category, i.e., bequest in meaning. Since the third category, i.e., bequest in reality, is clearer, he did not mention an example for it. 'And quality is evaluated' etc.: such that the sick person sells good quality property for bad quality property of the same type to an heir to convey the benefit of quality to his heir, because quality—although not considered at the time of selling good for bad of the same type, like selling good wheat for bad wheat—yet this quality becomes evaluated when the sick person sells to his heir to ward off suspicion; this sale is not permissible, just as it becomes evaluated when a guardian or father sells property of a minor to himself of the same type to ward off harm, so his sale is not valid. This is an example of the fourth category.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the legal capacity of a sick person (marīḍ) regarding bequests and transactions with heirs. The commentary explains that a sick person is interdicted from disposing more than one-third of his wealth due to the rights of heirs and creditors. The Prophet's hadith from Sa'd establishes the one-third limit. The text then details four categories of transactions that may be invalidated: (1) sale of an asset to an heir for equivalent value (form), (2) acknowledgment of a debt or gift to an heir without compensation (meaning), (3) direct bequest to an heir (reality), and (4) sale of good quality goods for bad quality similar goods to an heir (doubt). According to Abu Hanifa, all such transactions are invalid regardless of value, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) permit some at equivalent value. The commentary also discusses the abrogation of the obligatory bequest by the inheritance verses. The hadith 'no bequest to an heir' is cited as primary proof.

Key Terms
الوصية (al-waṣiyya): Bequest or will; a disposition of property after death.
الحجر (al-ḥajr): Interdiction; legal restriction on disposing property due to illness.
الصورة والمعنى (al-ṣūra wa al-maʿnā): Form and meaning; two aspects of a transaction: its external legal form and its underlying intent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which hadith is cited to prove the prohibition of bequest to an heir?
A) Hadith of Sa'd about one-third
B) Hadith of Abu Umamah 'no bequest to an heir'
C) Hadith of 'the hand that gives is better'
D) Hadith of 'beware of injustice'

Key Takeaway
A bequest to an heir is invalid because Allah has fixed each heir's share, and a sick person cannot override this divine allocation.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

Form and Meaning: The Sick Person’s Transactions with Heirs
Classifies transactions that are invalid due to being disguised bequests to heirs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four categories of invalid transactions (form, meaning, reality, doubt) and evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a sale at equivalent value.

Core Idea
A sale from a sick person to an heir is invalid regardless of price, because the heir's right attaches to the specific asset, not just its value.

This lesson delves into the detailed classification of a sick person's acts that are considered disguised bequests. The passage categorizes transactions into those that are invalid in form, meaning, reality, and doubt. It also presents the well-known dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on whether a sale at fair market value is permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
List the categories mentioned in the text: (1) form – sale of an asset to an heir at equivalent value; (2) meaning – acknowledgment (iqrar) to an heir without compensation; (3) reality – direct bequest to an heir; (4) doubt – sale of good quality goods for bad quality similar goods to an heir.
The text gives examples for each except reality, which is obvious.
2
Understand the rationale for invalidity
According to Abu Hanifa, all these transactions are invalid because they are disguised bequests. The heir's right attaches to the specific asset (al-ʻayniyya) due to potential inheritance, so any transfer of that asset to the heir by any means is essentially a bequest.
The Sahibayn limit the invalidity to cases where the transaction does not preserve the financial value.
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf on sale at equivalent value
Compare Abu Hanifa's absolute invalidity (even at fair market price) with the Sahibayn's view that a sale at equivalent value is valid because it does not harm other heirs.
The thamarah al-khilaf: in practice, a sick person selling his house to his son for market price is invalid per Abu Hanifa, valid per Sahibayn.
4
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a scenario of a sick person gifting an asset to an heir or selling at undervalue, determine the ruling according to the mufta bihi position (Abu Hanifa).
The mufta bihi is the stricter view for the sake of protecting heirs' rights.

عوارض سماوية والوصية بذلك إلا أن الشرع جوّز ذلك من الثلث نظراً له، ولما تولى الشرع الإيصاء لكن أي من ثلث ماله للورثة، وأبطل إيصاءه لهم بطل ذلك صورة ومعنى أي نسخ أي المريض يعودني، فقلت: والوصية بذلك : أي لا يملك الوصية بالصلة وأداء الحقوق، وذلك؛ لأن سبب الحجر وهو المرض باعتبار تعلق حق الوارث والغريم موجود، فكيف يملك المريض تلك الأمور. نظراً له لأن الإنسان بسبب طول أمله قاصر عن العمل ويقول: سوف أفعل هذا، فإذا أدركه الأجل احتاج إلى تلافي ما فرط في العمل، وهو في تلك الحالة لا يقدر على أداء العبادات البدنية، فرخص له أن يتصدق بثلث ماله ليتدارك بعض ما قصر فيه، وقد روى الترمذي عن سعد أنه قال: "مرضت" عام الفتح مرضاً أشفيت منه على الموت، فأتاني رسول الله له بن أبي وقاص يا رسول الله ! إن لي مالاً كثيراً وليس يرثني إلا ابنتي فأوصي بمالي كله؟ قال: لا، قلت: فثلثي مالي؟ قال لا، :قلت فالشطر؟ قال: لا قلت فالثلث؟ قال: الثلث والثلث كثير. تولى إلخ. الشرع إلخ: بنفسه ولم يفوّضه إلى المريض حيث قال: يُوصِيكُمُ اللَّهُ فِي أَوْلادِكُمْ ﴾ (النساء: ١١). لهم: أي للورثة، وقد كان الوصية في ابتداء الإسلام مفروضة بقوله تعالى: كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمْ إِذَا حَضَرَ أَحَدَكُمُ الْمَوْتُ ﴾ (البقرة: ۱۸٠)، فنسخت بقوله تعالى يُوصِيكُمُ اللهُ ، فالوصية كانت في ابتداء الإسلام مفروضة إذا لم تكن حق الورثة مقرّراً في كتاب الله تعالى، ولما كان العبد عاجزاً عن تعين مقدار ما يوصى به لكل واحد، وقد كان يقع المضارة للبعض فيه حيث أشار إليه بقوله - جل ذكره - وغير مُضار، وبقوله ولا تَدْرُونَ أَيُّهُمْ أَقْرَبُ لَكُمْ نَفْعان﴾ (النساء: ۱۱)، أنزل آية الميراث وهي يوصيكم الله الآية وتولى الإيصاء بنفسه. بطل ذلك: أي إيصاء المريض للوارث، وقد روى الترمذي وغيره عن أبي أمامة الباهلى قال: سمعت رسول الله الله يقول في خطبته عام حجة الوداع: "إن الله تبارك وتعالى قد أعطى لكل ذي حق حقه، فلا وصية لوارث". الترمذي رقم: ۲۱۲۰] وقال هذا حديث حسن صورة: بأن يبيع المريض عيناً من التركة من الوارث بمثل القيمة، فهذه وصيته بصورة العين حيث أثر الوارث بعين من أعيان ماله لا معنى لاسترداد العوض منه، فلا يجوز أبي حنيفة حاله مطلقاً سواء كان بمثل القيمة أو لا، لأن حق الوارث كما يتعلّق بالمالية يتعلق بالعينية أيضاً، وعندهما يصح بمثل القيمة لعدم بطلان حق الورثة عن شيء مما يتعلق به حقهم. ومعنى: بأن يقر لأحد الورثة فإنه وصية معنى حيث يقرّ تسليم المقر به للمقر له بلا عوض، وهذا لايصح عندنا خلافا للشافعي له؛ لأن في إقراره لبعض الورثة تهمة الكذب؛ إذ من الجائز أن يكون غرضه إيصال المال إلى الوارث بغير عوض، وشبهة الحرام حرام فيكون حراماً ، فهذه وصية معنى وإقرار صورة. وحقيقة وشبهة حتى لا يصح بيعه من الوارث أصلاً عند أبي حنيفة له، وبطل إقراره له أي للوارث وإن حصل باستيفاء دين الصحة، وتقومت الجودة في حقهم كما تقومت في حق الصغار. إقراره وحقيقة بأن أوصى لأحد الورثة وشبهة بأن باع الجيد من الأموال الربوية بالردي منها. أصلا سواء كان بمثل القيمة أو لا. عند أبي حنيفة له: هذا مثال للقسم الأول، وهو الوصية صورة. دين الصحة: الذي كان له على الوارث بأن يقر المريض بأني حصلت من فلان الوارث ديني الذي كان عليه في حالة الصحة، وذلك لأن في إقراره بالاستيفاء تهمة أنه يريد إسقاط الدين عن ذمته، فكأنه يملك بهذا المقدار من المال، وهذا مثال للقسم الثاني وهو الوصية معنى ولما كان القسم الثالث وهو الوصية حقيقة أظهر، لم يذكر له مثالا. وتقومت الجودة إلخ بأن باع المريض مالاً جيداً بمال ردي من جنسه من وارث ليبلغ نفع الجودة إلى وارثه؛ لأن الجودة وإن لم يعتبر بها وقت بيع الجيد بالردي من جنسه كبيع الحنطة الجيدة بالحنطة الردية ولكن تتقوم هذه الجودة إذا باع المريض بوارثه دفعاً للتهمة لا يجوز هذا البيع، كما تتقوم إذا يبيع الوصي أو الأب مال الصغير من نفسه متجانساً دفعاً للضرر ، فلايصح بيعه، وهذا مثال للقسم الرابع.
Heavenly impediments. And the bequest to that [heir] is not permitted except that the Law permitted it from one-third as a consideration for him, and since the Law took charge of the bequest [by saying] 'but' i.e., from one-third of his wealth to the heirs, and invalidated his bequest to them, it becomes void in form and meaning, i.e., abrogated, i.e., the sick person [said] 'visit me', so I said: And the bequest to that: i.e., he does not have the power to bequeath for connection and fulfillment of rights, because the cause of interdiction—illness—considering the right of the heir and creditor exists, so how can the sick person own those matters? As a consideration for him, because man due to length of hope is deficient in action and says: I will do this later, so when death overtakes him he needs to make up for what he neglected in action, and in that state he is unable to perform physical acts of worship, so a concession was given for him to give charity from one-third of his wealth to compensate for some of his deficiency. And al-Tirmidhi narrated from Sa'd that he said: 'I fell ill in the year of conquest with an illness from which I almost died, so the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) visited me. I said: O Messenger of Allah, I have much wealth and only a daughter inherits from me, so should I bequeath all my wealth? He said: No. I said: Two-thirds? He said: No. I said: Half? He said: No. I said: One-third? He said: One-third, and one-third is much.' [Tirmidhi no. 2116] And since it might be thought that when the Law permitted bequest from one-third as a consideration for him, one knows that one-third is his exclusive right not related to anyone else's right so that his disposition in it is permissible, whether he bequeaths to an heir or to others, he removed that [misunderstanding] by his saying: 'Since the Law took charge...' etc. The Law etc.: by itself and did not delegate it to the sick person where He said: 'Allah instructs you concerning your children' (4:11). 'For them': i.e., for the heirs. And bequest at the beginning of Islam was obligatory by His statement: 'Prescribed for you, when death approaches one of you' (2:180), then it was abrogated by His statement 'Allah instructs you'. So bequest was obligatory at the beginning of Islam when the right of heirs was not established in Allah's Book. And since the servant is incapable of determining the amount to be bequeathed to each one, and harm used to occur to some, He indicated by His statement 'without harm' and 'you do not know which of them is nearer to you in benefit' (4:11), He revealed the verse of inheritance, i.e., 'Allah instructs you' verse, and took charge of bequest Himself. 'That becomes void': i.e., the sick person's bequest to the heir. And al-Tirmidhi and others narrated from Abu Umamah al-Bahili that he said: I heard the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) say in his sermon during the Farewell Pilgrimage: 'Indeed Allah the Blessed and Exalted has given every rightful person his right, so no bequest to an heir.' [Tirmidhi no. 2120] He said: this is a hasan hadith. 'Form': such that the sick person sells a specific asset from the estate to the heir for an equivalent value, so this is his bequest in the form of an asset where he benefits the heir with a specific asset from his property without meaning to retrieve the consideration, so it is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa in all cases, whether for equivalent value or not, because the heir's right is related to the specific asset as well as to the financial value. According to the two, it is valid for equivalent value because the right of the heirs is not invalidated regarding anything related to their right. 'Meaning': such that he acknowledges (iqrar) for one of the heirs, for it is a bequest in meaning where he acknowledges delivery of the acknowledged item to the donee without compensation, and this is not valid according to us contrary to al-Shafi'i, because in his acknowledgment to some heirs there is suspicion of lying, as it is possible that his aim is to deliver wealth to the heir without compensation, and the suspicion of the forbidden is forbidden, so it is forbidden. So this is a bequest in meaning and an acknowledgment in form. 'Reality and doubt' so that his sale to the heir is not valid at all according to Abu Hanifa, and his acknowledgment to him—i.e., to the heir—becomes void even if obtained through collection of a healthy debt, and quality is evaluated regarding them as it is evaluated regarding minors. His acknowledgment: 'Reality' such that he bequeaths to one of the heirs, and 'doubt' such that he sells good quality goods from ribawi assets for bad quality ones. Absolutely, whether for equivalent value or not. According to Abu Hanifa: this is an example of the first category, which is bequest in form. 'Healthy debt': that which was owed to him from the heir, such that the sick person acknowledges that I collected from so-and-so the heir my debt that was upon him in a state of health, because in his acknowledgment of collection there is suspicion that he wants to drop the debt from his liability, as if he gives him ownership of this amount of wealth. This is an example of the second category, i.e., bequest in meaning. Since the third category, i.e., bequest in reality, is clearer, he did not mention an example for it. 'And quality is evaluated' etc.: such that the sick person sells good quality property for bad quality property of the same type to an heir to convey the benefit of quality to his heir, because quality—although not considered at the time of selling good for bad of the same type, like selling good wheat for bad wheat—yet this quality becomes evaluated when the sick person sells to his heir to ward off suspicion; this sale is not permissible, just as it becomes evaluated when a guardian or father sells property of a minor to himself of the same type to ward off harm, so his sale is not valid. This is an example of the fourth category.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the legal capacity of a sick person (marīḍ) regarding bequests and transactions with heirs. The commentary explains that a sick person is interdicted from disposing more than one-third of his wealth due to the rights of heirs and creditors. The Prophet's hadith from Sa'd establishes the one-third limit. The text then details four categories of transactions that may be invalidated: (1) sale of an asset to an heir for equivalent value (form), (2) acknowledgment of a debt or gift to an heir without compensation (meaning), (3) direct bequest to an heir (reality), and (4) sale of good quality goods for bad quality similar goods to an heir (doubt). According to Abu Hanifa, all such transactions are invalid regardless of value, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) permit some at equivalent value. The commentary also discusses the abrogation of the obligatory bequest by the inheritance verses. The hadith 'no bequest to an heir' is cited as primary proof.

Key Terms
الوصية (al-waṣiyya): Bequest or will; a disposition of property after death.
الحجر (al-ḥajr): Interdiction; legal restriction on disposing property due to illness.
الصورة والمعنى (al-ṣūra wa al-maʿnā): Form and meaning; two aspects of a transaction: its external legal form and its underlying intent.
الإقرار (al-iqrār): Acknowledgment; a declaration that another person has a right against the declarant.
الشبهة (al-shubha): Suspicion or doubt; a transaction that resembles a valid one but has an invalidating element.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling of a sick person's acknowledgment (iqrar) to an heir without proof?
A) Valid, because it is a form of debt repayment
B) Invalid, because it is a bequest in meaning
C) Valid only if the heir is a minor
D) Invalid only if the amount is large

Key Takeaway
Any transaction between a sick person and an heir that transfers a specific asset or benefit is considered a disguised bequest and is invalid, especially according to Abu Hanifa.

Menstruation and Legal Capacity: A Condition, Not a Removal
Explains effect of hayd/nifas on ahliyya and worship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how menstruation (hayd) affects legal capacity (ahliyya) in Hanafi usul, distinguish between capacity and condition (shart), and explain why the hadith on fasting overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
Menstruation does not remove legal capacity but makes purity a condition for prayer and fasting — a ruling established by hadith, not analogy.

In this passage, the author clarifies a common misunderstanding: menstruation does not strip a woman of her legal capacity (ahliyya). Instead, it creates a condition (shart) of purity for the validity of prayer and fasting. The proofs come from authentic hadith, one of which (on fasting) overrides an otherwise valid qiyas. We will walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The text states that hayd and nifas do not remove ahliyya (legal capacity) in any way. Understand that ahliyya includes both capacity for obligation (wujub) and capacity for performance (ada).
This is based on the fact that these conditions do not affect the dhimma, intellect, or physical ability.
2
Examine the condition (shart)
Purity from hayd/nifas is a condition for the validity of prayer and fasting. This means the acts are not valid without it, but the obligation remains.
The shart affects ada (performance), not wujub (obligation).
3
Analyze the proof for prayer
The hadith of Fatimah bint Qais (Bukhari/Muslim) shows the Prophet commanding women to leave prayer during menstruation and resume after purity. This establishes the condition.
The hadith distinguishes hayd from istihadah, indicating that only hayd prevents prayer.
4
Analyze the proof for fasting
The hadith of Aisha (Tirmidhi) indicates that women did not fast during menstruation and were commanded to make up the fasts. This shows purity is a condition for fasting, and that fasting must be made up.
The hadith also shows that prayer is not made up, highlighting a difference.
5
Understand the role of qiyas
The text notes that the ruling on fasting is contrary to qiyas. Analogically, fasting should be valid during menstruation as it is valid during janabah. However, the explicit hadith overrides qiyas.
This demonstrates the principle: a specific textual proof (nass) takes precedence over analogical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحيض والنفاس فإنهما لا يعدمان أهليةً بوجه ما لكن الطهارة عنهما شرط لجواز أداء الصوم والصلاة، أي الحيض والنفاس جمعهما لتشابهما صورةً وحكما أهلية: لا أهلية الوجوب ولا أهلية الأداء. بوجه ما وذلك لأنهما لا يخلان بالذمة والعقل وقدرة البدن، ولما كان يتوهم أنه فعلي هذا ينبغي أن لا يسقط بهماء قضاء الصلاة والصلاة: أما للصلاة فقد روى البخاري والمسلم أن فاطمة بنت قيس قالت: يا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إلى امرأة أستحاض فلا أطهر، أفادع الصلاة؟ فقال: لا، إنما ذلك عرق وليس بحيض، فإذا أقبلت حيضتك فدعي الصلاة، وإذا أدبرت فاغسلي عنك الدم ثم صلّي [البخاري، رقم: ۲۲۸ ، مسلم، رقم:٧٥٣] وهذا موافق للقياس وأما للصوم فقد روى الترمذي عن عائشة ما قالت: كنا نحيض عند رسول الله لهم ثم نطهر، فيأمرنا بقضاء الصيام ولا يأمرنا بقضاء الصلاة. وقال: هذا حديث حسن [الترمذي، رقم: ۷۸۷] وكذا روى مسلم عنها بمعناه، فعلم من هذا الحديث :أمران أحدهما أن النساء ما كن يصمن في حالة الحيض، فثبت أن الطهارة عن الحيض شرط للصوم، وهو مخالف للقياس؛ إذ القياس أن يتأدّى الصوم مع الحيض كما يتأدّى مع الجنابة، وثانيهما أنه لا قضاء للصلاة والصوم قضاء، ولما كان الطهارة عن الحيض والنفاس شرطاً لأداء الصوم والصلاة. رضي
As for menstruation and postpartum bleeding, they do not remove legal capacity in any way, but purity from them is a condition for the validity of performing fasting and prayer. That is, menstruation and postpartum bleeding are combined due to their similarity in form and ruling. Legal capacity: neither capacity for obligation nor capacity for performance is removed in any way, because they do not affect the liability (dhimmah), intellect, or physical ability. And since it might be thought that based on this, the makeup of prayer and fasting should not be waived by them. As for prayer, al-Bukhari and Muslim narrated that Fatimah bint Qais said: 'O Messenger of Allah, I am a woman who has istihadah (irregular bleeding) and I do not become pure, should I abandon prayer?' He said: 'No, that is only a vein and not menstruation. When your menstruation comes, leave prayer, and when it ends, wash the blood from you and pray.' [Bukhari no. 228, Muslim no. 753] And this is in accordance with analogy. As for fasting, al-Tirmidhi narrated from Aisha that she said: 'We used to menstruate in the time of the Messenger of Allah, then we would become pure, and he would command us to make up fasting and not command us to make up prayer.' He said: this is hasan hadith. [Tirmidhi no. 787] Similarly, Muslim narrated from her its meaning. From this hadith two things are learned: one, that women did not fast during menstruation, so it is established that purity from menstruation is a condition for fasting, and this is contrary to analogy, because analogy would require that fasting be valid during menstruation as it is valid during janabah (major impurity); the second, that there is no makeup for prayer but there is makeup for fasting. And since purity from menstruation and postpartum bleeding is a condition for performing fasting and prayer. May Allah be pleased.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the effect of menstruation (ḥayḍ) and postpartum bleeding (nifās) on legal capacity (ahliyya). The commentary emphasizes that these conditions do not affect capacity for obligation (wujūb) or capacity for performance (adā') because they do not involve the mind, maturity, or physical ability. However, purity from them is a condition for the validity of prayer and fasting. The proof for prayer comes from the hadith of Fatimah bint Qais, which distinguishes between menstruation and istihadah, showing that menstruation prevents prayer. For fasting, the hadith of Aisha indicates that women did not fast during menstruation and were required to make up the fasts but not the prayers. The commentary notes that the ruling for fasting is against qiyas (analogy) because, analogically, fasting should be valid during menstruation just as it is during janabah, but the hadith overrides analogy. Thus, menstruation blocks prayer and fasting but does not eliminate the obligation of makeup for fasting, while prayer requires no makeup.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-ḥayḍ): Menstruation; a natural condition that prevents valid prayer and fasting.
النفاس (al-nifās): Postpartum bleeding; same rulings as menstruation regarding prayer and fasting.
الأهلية (al-ahliyya): Legal capacity; the fitness of a person to acquire rights and duties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary difference between the rulings on prayer and fasting during menstruation according to the hadith cited?
A) Both require makeup.
B) Prayer requires makeup; fasting does not.
C) Fasting requires makeup; prayer does not.
D) Neither requires makeup.

Key Takeaway
Menstruation and postpartum bleeding do not remove legal capacity but impose a condition of purity for worship, with the hadith on fasting overriding analogy and requiring makeup.

Haqiqa and Shubha in Sick Person's Transactions with Heirs
Examples of haqiqa and shubha in sick person's dealings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the categories of haqiqa and shubha in a sick person's transactions with heirs and apply the Hanafi rule that such transactions are invalid.

Core Idea
A sick person's direct or indirect benefit to an heir is invalid to prevent collusion against creditors.

In Hanafi usul, the concepts of haqiqa and shubha help classify a sick person's dealings with his heirs. A direct bequest (haqiqa) and a transaction that indirectly benefits the heir (shubha) are both invalid under Abu Hanifa to protect the rights of creditors and other heirs. This lesson examines the examples given in the text and the reasoning behind the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories: haqiqa and shubha
Understand that haqiqa refers to a literal will or gift to an heir, while shubha refers to a transaction that resembles a benefit, such as selling good quality goods for bad.
Both are invalid according to Abu Hanifa.
2
Analyze the example of haqiqa: a bequest to an heir
The text gives the example of a sick person making a will to an heir. This is a direct benefit.
This is invalid at the time of death because it deprives other heirs or creditors.
3
Analyze the example of shubha: selling good quality ribawi goods for bad
Even if the sale is at market value, the exchange of quality creates a benefit to the heir. The text says quality is considered here.
This is similar to a guardian's sale to himself of a minor's property.
4
Understand the third and fourth categories: acknowledgment of debt and the quality issue
The text also covers a sick person acknowledging receipt of a debt from an heir (constructive will), and the ruling that quality is considered when selling to an heir to prevent suspicion.
The fourth category is the consideration of quality in homogeneous goods.
5
Apply the rule: under Abu Hanifa, all such transactions are invalid
The overarching principle is to prevent collusion against creditors. Any transaction that could be a disguised gift to an heir is void.
This is a strict position to protect creditors' rights.

وحقيقة وشبهة حتى لا يصح بيعه من الوارث أصلاً عند أبي حنيفة له، وبطل إقراره له أي للوارث وإن حصل باستيفاء دين الصحة، وتقومت الجودة في حقهم كما تقومت في حق الصغار. إقراره وحقيقة بأن أوصى لأحد الورثة وشبهة بأن باع الجيد من الأموال الربوية بالردي منها. أصلا سواء كان بمثل القيمة أو لا. عند أبي حنيفة له: هذا مثال للقسم الأول، وهو الوصية صورة. دين الصحة: الذي كان له على الوارث بأن يقر المريض بأني حصلت من فلان الوارث ديني الذي كان عليه في حالة الصحة، وذلك لأن في إقراره بالاستيفاء تهمة أنه يريد إسقاط الدين عن ذمته، فكأنه يملك بهذا المقدار من المال، وهذا مثال للقسم الثاني وهو الوصية معنى ولما كان القسم الثالث وهو الوصية حقيقة أظهر، لم يذكر له مثالا. وتقومت الجودة إلخ بأن باع المريض مالاً جيداً بمال ردي من جنسه من وارث ليبلغ نفع الجودة إلى وارثه؛ لأن الجودة وإن لم يعتبر بها وقت بيع الجيد بالردي من جنسه كبيع الحنطة الجيدة بالحنطة الردية ولكن تتقوم هذه الجودة إذا باع المريض بوارثه دفعاً للتهمة لا يجوز هذا البيع، كما تتقوم إذا يبيع الوصي أو الأب مال الصغير من نفسه متجانساً دفعاً للضرر، فلايصح بيعه، وهذا مثال للقسم الرابع.
And [there is] reality and semblance so that his sale from the heir is not valid at all according to Abu Hanifa, and his acknowledgment is void for him, i.e., for the heir, even if it occurs by receiving the debt of health, and the quality is considered in their right just as it is considered in the right of minors. His acknowledgment. And reality is that he bequeaths to one of the heirs, and semblance is that he sells good quality ribawi goods for bad quality ones, either equal in value or not. According to Abu Hanifa: this is an example of the first category, which is a will in form. The debt of health: that which he had against the heir, by the sick person acknowledging that he received from such-and-such heir the debt that was upon him in the state of health, because in his acknowledgment of receipt there is suspicion that he wants to drop the debt from his liability, as if he owns this amount of wealth, and this is an example of the second category, which is a will in meaning. And since the third category is a will in reality and is clearer, he did not mention an example for it. And 'quality is considered' etc., is that the sick person sells good quality property for bad quality property of its type from an heir to convey the benefit of quality to his heir; because although quality is not taken into account at the time of selling good for bad of its type, like selling good wheat for bad wheat, this quality is considered when the sick person sells to his heir to prevent suspicion, so this sale is not permissible, just as it is considered when a guardian or father sells the property of a minor from himself as same type to prevent harm, so his sale is not valid, and this is an example of the fourth category.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on rulings regarding a sick person's transactions with his heirs. It distinguishes between haqiqa (reality) and shubha (semblance) in such transactions, explaining that both prevent validity. The text gives examples: a will to an heir is haqiqa; acknowledging a debt from an heir is a will in meaning; selling good quality goods for bad quality is shubha. Under Abu Hanifa, these are invalid due to suspicion that the sick person intends to benefit his heir at the expense of other heirs or creditors. The commentary also discusses the concept of 'quality being considered' when selling to an heir, similar to how a guardian must consider quality when selling a minor's property to himself.

Key Terms
حقيقة (ḥaqīqa): A transaction that is literally a will or gift to an heir.
شبهة (shubha): A transaction that resembles a forbidden benefit to an heir, such as selling good quality goods for bad.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment of a debt; here, a sick person’s acknowledgment of receiving a debt from an heir is treated as a constructive will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the heir is not trustworthy
B) Because of suspicion that the sick person intends to cancel the debt and benefit the heir
C) Because the sick person is not qualified to acknowledge
D) Because the debt is not proven

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, any transaction by a terminally ill person that directly or indirectly benefits an heir is invalid to prevent collusion against creditors.

Death as Pure Inability: Removing Taklif Obligations
Defines death as pure inability and its effect on taklif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why death is considered pure inability in Hanafi usul, state its effect on taklif obligations, and classify the four types of post-death rulings.

Core Idea
Death is pure inability: no trace of capability remains, so all obligations of performance fall away.

This passage defines death as a heavenly impediment that completely removes taklif obligations because the purpose of taklif—voluntary performance—becomes impossible. It also compares death with other impediments where some capability remains, and provides a systematic classification of rulings that persist after death.

Guided Path
1
Define death in legal terms
The passage states death is 'pure inability' (عجز خالص), the separation of soul from body, and the last heavenly impediment. There is a theological debate about whether death is existential or non-existential, but the legal focus is on its effect on capability.
Preserve the definition: death has no aspect of capability whatsoever.
2
Identify the rationale for falling of taklif
The purpose of taklif is performance by choice (الأداء عن اختيار). Death makes voluntary performance impossible, so the obligation falls away due to loss of purpose.
This is a rational (aqli) proof: since the purpose cannot be achieved, the obligation is lifted.
3
Distinguish death from other impediments
Illness, minority, insanity, and slavery all involve some remaining ability. Death is unique: it is 'pure inability' with no aspect of capability.
The passage explicitly contrasts death with these impediments to highlight its unique legal effect.
4
Analyze the theological debate on death's nature
Some scholars consider death an existential attribute (based on Quran 67:2 and the hadith of the ram). Others consider it the absence of life. The commentator reconciles by defining death as separation of soul from body, not pure non-existence.
This debate does not affect the legal ruling that taklif falls away.
5
Classify post-death rulings
Worldly rulings are of four types: (1) taklif obligations (prayer, zakat) – they drop entirely, except sin may persist; (2) rulings for the needs of others; (3) rulings for the deceased's own need – persist if they fulfill that need; (4) things that do not serve a need – go to heirs. Hereafter rulings are permanent.
This classification is essential for understanding what obligations remain on the estate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الموت فإنه عجز خالص يسقط به ما هو من باب التكليف لفوات غرضه، وهو الأداء عن اختيار، أي بالعجز الحاصل بعد الموت. الموت وهو آخر العوارض السماوية، واختلف في تفسيره فقال كثير من أهل السنة: هو صفة وجودية خلقت ضداً للحياة؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿الَّذِي خَلَقَ الْمَوْتَ وَالْحَيَاةَ (الملك: ٢)، ولقوله: 'يؤتى بالموت يوم القيمة في صورة كبش أملح فيذبح'، [الترمذي، رقم: ٣١٥٦] وعلى هذا صح عده من العوارض السماوية، وقيل: هو عدم الحياة عما شأنه الحياة معنى الخلق في الآية من التقدير، وإليه ذهب صاحب المسلّم حيث قال في 'منهية المسلّم: وجعله وجودياً ليس بشيء، وإنما تعلّق الخلق به؛ لأنه عدم طار. أقول: ليس المراد من كونه عدمياً أنه عدم محض وفناء صرف، بل هو مفارقة الروح عن البدن وانتقاله من دار إلى دار، ولذا يعد الميت في أحكام الآخرة حياً فاحفظه عجز خالص: ليس فيه جهة القدرة بوجه ما، واحترز به عن المرض والصغر والجنون والرق فإن العجز بهذه العوارض متحقق، ولكن يبقى جهة القدرة بخلاف الموت، ثم الأحكام المتعلقة بالميت إما أحكام الدنيا وإما أحكام الآخرة، ثم أحكام الدنيا على أربعة أقسام منها ما هو من باب التكليف كوجوب الصلاة والزكاة، وحكمه السقوط إلا في حق الإثم، ومنها ما شرع عليه لحاجة الغير وهو عدة أقسام، ومنها ما شرع الحاجة نفسه، وحكمه أن يبقى ما يقضى به الحاجة، ومنها ما لا يصلح لقضاء حاجته، وحكمه أن يثبت للورثة. وأما الأحكام الأخروية فحكمها البقاء. باب التكليف بيان للقسم الأول من أحكام الدنيا عن اختيار والحاصل أن الغرض من التكليف بالنسبة إلى المكلّف هو إتيان المكلّف به عن اختيار، وبالموت تحقق العجز اللازم الذي لا يرجى زواله، ولا عجز فوقه.
As for death, it is pure inability by which that which is of the category of taklif (obligation) falls away due to the loss of its purpose, which is performance with choice. Death is the last of the heavenly impediments. There is disagreement in its interpretation: many of the Ahl al-Sunnah said it is an existential attribute created as the opposite of life, based on His statement: 'Who created death and life' (Mulk: 2), and his statement: 'Death will be brought on the Day of Resurrection in the form of a white-furred ram and will be slaughtered.' [Tirmidhi 3156]. On this view, it is correctly counted among the heavenly impediments. It was also said: it is the absence of life from that which is typically alive, with the meaning of 'creation' in the verse being predestination. This is the view of the author of al-Musallam, who said in 'Munyat al-Muslim': 'Considering it existential is nothing; creation is attributed to it only because it is an accidental non-existence.' I say: The intent of its being non-existential is not that it is pure non-existence and mere annihilation, but rather it is the separation of the soul from the body and its transfer from one abode to another; therefore the deceased is considered alive in matters of the Hereafter, so preserve this. Pure inability: there is no aspect of ability in it whatsoever, distinguished from illness, minority, insanity, and slavery, for inability in these impediments is realized, but the aspect of ability remains, unlike death. Then the rulings pertaining to the deceased are either rulings of this world or of the Hereafter. The rulings of this world are of four types: among them is what is of the category of taklif, like obligation of prayer and zakat, and its ruling is that it falls away except regarding sin; among them is what is legislated for the need of others, which has several types; among them is what is legislated for his own need, and its ruling is that what fulfills his need remains; among them is what is not suitable for fulfilling his need, and its ruling is that it goes to the heirs. As for the rulings of the Hereafter, their ruling is permanence. The section on taklif explains the first type of the rulings of this world. The conclusion is that the purpose of taklif with respect to the obligated person is the performance of the obligation by choice, and with death, the necessary inability that cannot be hoped to be removed is realized, and there is no inability beyond it.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a definition and analysis of death as a heavenly impediment ('arid samawi). It begins by stating that death is 'pure inability' (عجز خالص) which removes all taklif obligations because the purpose of taklif—voluntary performance—cannot be realized after death. The commentator discusses a theological dispute: whether death is an existent being created by Allah (supported by Quran and hadith that death will be slaughtered) or a mere absence of life. The commentator prefers the view that death is the separation of soul from body, not pure non-existence. He then compares death to other impediments (illness, minority, insanity, slavery) where some ability remains, unlike death. The passage then outlines the four categories of worldly rulings that apply to a deceased person: (1) taklif obligations (like prayer/zakat) drop entirely (though sin may persist); (2) those for others' needs; (3) those for his own need remain if they satisfy that need; (4) those not needed go to heirs. Hereafter rulings persist. The key takeaway is that death eliminates all obligations of performance because voluntary action is impossible.

Key Terms
عجز خالص (ʿajz khāliṣ): Pure inability with no aspect of capability whatsoever, unique to death.
تكليف (taklīf): The obligation to perform an act by choice; its purpose is voluntary performance.
عوارض سماوية (ʿawāriḍ samāwiyya): Heavenly impediments that affect legal capacity from above, not from human choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the deceased is no longer capable of earning reward.
B) Because voluntary performance is impossible due to pure inability.
C) Because Allah forgives all sins after death.
D) Because the body disintegrates.

Key Takeaway
Death is pure inability, removing all obligations of performance; only obligations that are financial or serve the needs of others may persist.

Celestial Accidents and Their Effect on Obligations
Usul definition of celestial accidents with talil for prayer vs. fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define celestial accidents ('awariḍ samawiyya), explain why hayd and nifas remove the prayer obligation but not the fasting obligation, and apply the principle of haraj to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Hayd and nifas remove prayer but not fasting because making up prayer causes haraj, while fasting does not.

In this lesson, we examine the concept of 'celestial accidents' (al-'awariḍ al-samawiyya) as defined in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. These are natural conditions that affect legal capacity without culpability. The author uses menstruation (hayd) and postpartum bleeding (nifas) as primary examples to illustrate how hardship (haraj) can lead to the complete removal of an original obligation (asl al-ṣalāh) while another obligation (asl al-ṣawm) remains intact. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the maxim that obligations are lifted to prevent extreme difficulty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition
Locate the term 'awariḍ samawiyya and note that it is defined by examples: hayd and nifas. Understand that these are conditions outside a person's control that modify or remove obligations.
The term is translated as 'celestial accidents' because they are seen as divinely ordained events.
2
Understand the Impact on Performance (Adā')
The text states 'performance is lost' (yafūt al-adā'). This means that during the days of hayd/nifas, the woman cannot perform the prayer validly because purity is lacking.
Adā' refers to performing an act at its prescribed time.
3
Analyze the Difference in Make-up (Qadā')
The key distinction lies in qadā'. For prayer, making up all missed prayers would be extremely burdensome (haraj) because prayers multiply daily. For fasting, only a few days are missed, so no haraj exists. Hence, the original obligation (asl) of prayer is removed, but that of fasting is not.
Haraj is a recognized legal cause (sabab) for lifting obligations, based on Quran 22:78 and the maxim 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysīr'.
4
Apply the Principle to Other Cases
The same reasoning applies to other 'celestial accidents' like insanity or unconsciousness. Use the presence or absence of haraj to determine whether an obligation is completely lifted or merely postponed.
For example, a person unconscious for weeks: prayer obligation may be lifted due to haraj, but fasting days are few, so they still owe qadā'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القياس عوارض سماوية أي بالحيض والنفاس فيفوت الأداء بهما، وفي قضاء الصلاة حرج لتضاعفها، فسقط بهما أصل الصلاة، بعد أيام الحيض ولا حرج في قضاء الصوم فلم يسقط أصله.
Chapter of Qiyas. Celestial accidents: that is, by menstruation and postpartum bleeding. Performance is lost due to them, and in making up prayer there is hardship because they multiply, so the original obligation of prayer is removed by them, after the days of menstruation, and there is no hardship in making up fasting, so its original obligation is not removed.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage defines celestial accidents ('awariḍ samawiyya) using the examples of hayd and nifas. The author explains that these conditions cause loss of the ability to perform (ada') the prayer because purity is lacking. However, the key difference between prayer and fasting is in their makeup (qada'): making up numerous prayers throughout the year would be extremely burdensome (haraj), so the original obligation of prayer is entirely dropped for these days. In contrast, fasting is only missed for a few days and can be made up with ease, so its obligation remains. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that hardship (haraj) leads to the removal of obligations.

Key Terms
عوارض سماوية ('awariḍ samawiyya): Accidental conditions arising from natural/supernatural causes that affect legal capacity.
حرج (haraj): Extreme difficulty or hardship that the Law considers unacceptable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because women are excused from prayer during these days but not from fasting.
D) Because fasting is easier to perform than prayer.

Key Takeaway
The difference between prayer and fasting in hayd/nifas is due to the presence or absence of hardship (haraj) in making up the missed acts.

Death as Pure Incapacity: Legal Effects in Hanafi Usul
Analyzing death as a celestial accident and its legal effects on capacity and obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why death is classified as pure incapacity ('ajz khalis) and apply the Hanafi rulings on survival of obligations and debts after death.

Core Idea
Death removes all capacity for performance, but sin remains; debts persist only with wealth or guarantee.

In this passage, the author defines death as 'pure incapacity' ('ajz khalis) and explores its legal consequences. Understanding this definition is key to grasping how Hanafi scholars categorize the effects of death on worship obligations, financial rights, and debts. The text also highlights a significant intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding suretyship for the deceased.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of death as pure incapacity
Recognize that death removes all ability, distinguishing it from other states like illness or madness where some capacity remains.
This is foundational for all subsequent rulings.
2
Categorize the rulings for the deceased
The passage divides rulings into worldly (four categories) and Hereafter. Identify each category.
Use the author's classification as a framework.
3
Analyze the rationale for lifting obligations of worship
The purpose of obligation is performance with choice; death frustrates that purpose, so obligations drop, but sin for missed performance persists.
This shows the link between purpose (gharaḍ) and ruling.
4
Examine the survival of rights and debts
Rights connected to specific assets remain; debts require estate or guarantor to persist. This leads to the dispute on kafala.
Contrast with the majority view.
5
Evaluate the evidence and the dispute
Consider the hadith cited by the majority and Abu Hanifa's response, understanding the thamarah al-khilaf.
Think about how textual interpretation affects legal conclusions.

بالميت إما أحكام الدنيا وإما أحكام الآخرة، ثم أحكام الدنيا على أربعة أقسام منها ما هو من باب التكليف كوجوب الصلاة والزكاة، وحكمه السقوط إلا في حق الإثم، ومنها ما شرع عليه لحاجة الغير وهو عدة أقسام، ومنها ما شرع الحاجة نفسه، وحكمه أن يبقى ما يقضى به الحاجة، ومنها ما لا يصلح لقضاء حاجته، وحكمه أن يثبت للورثة. وأما الأحكام الأخروية فحكمها البقاء. باب التكليف بيان للقسم الأول من أحكام الدنيا عن اختيار والحاصل أن الغرض من التكليف بالنسبة إلى المكلّف هو إتيان المكلّف به عن اختيار، وبالموت تحقق العجز اللازم الذي لا يرجى زواله، ولا عجز فوقه. ولهذا قلنا: إنه يبطل عنه الزكاة وسائر وجوه القرب، وإنما يبقى عليه المأثم، وما شرع عليه لحاجة غيره إن كان حقاً متعلقا بالعين يبقى ببقائه؛ لأن فعله فيه غير مقصود، وإن كان ديناً لم يبق بمجرد الذمة حتى ينضمّ إليه مال أو ما يؤكد به الذمم وهو ذمة الكفيل. ولهذا قال أبو حنيفة : إن الكفالة بالدين عن الميت لا تصح إذا لم يخلف مالاً أو كفيلاً، ولهذا: أي لفوات غرض التكليف وهو إتيان المكلف به عن اختيار. يبطل عنه إلخ أي يسقط عن الميت في حكم الدنيا حتى لا يجب أداؤها من التركة خلافاً للشافعي بناء على أن المقصود هو المال دون الفعل، حتى لو ظفر الفقير بمال الزكاة كان له أن يأخذ مقدار الزكاة فتسقط الزكاة عن ذمته وعندنا المقصود هو الفعل وقد فات بموته. وسائر وجوه إلخ عطف على قوله "الزكاة" أي يبطل عنه سائر وجوه القرب كالصلاة والصوم والحج. المأثم: لتقصيره في أدائه حين كان حياً صحيحاً، والإثم من أحكام الآخرة، والميت في أحكام الآخرة حي، فإن شاء الله عفا عنه بكرمه وفضله، وإن شاء عذبه بحكمته وعدله لحاجة غيره : هذا هو القسم الثاني، وهو على عدة أقسام كما قلنا الأوّل ما بينه بقوله: إن كان إلخ. بالعين يبقى إلخ أي العين كالمرهون يتعلّق به حق المرتهن، ولا يبطل بموت الراهن، وكذا المستأجر يتعلّق به حق المستاجر، والوديعة يتعلق بها حق المودع، والمبيع يتعلق به حق المشترى غير مقصود إذ المقصود في حقوق العباد هو المال، فيبقى حق العبد في العين بعد موته أيضا، يأخذه صاحب الحق أو لا من غير أن يدخل في التركة، ويقسم على الغرماء والورثة. إليه: أي إلى الذمة بتأويل المذكور ذمة الكفيل والحاصل أن الميت إذا لم يترك مالاً أو كفيلا من حضوره لايبقى دينه في الدنيا حتى يطالب من أولاده، وإنما يأخذ في الآخرة. ولهذا: أي لأجل أنه لم يبق في ذمة دين كفيلا لأن الكفالة ضمّ الذمة إلى الذمة، وإذا لم يبق للميت ذمة معتبرة، فكيف يصح ذمة الكفيل إليه. نعم لو كان له مال أو كفيل من حالة الحياة فإذن يصح الكفالة منه؛ لأن ذمته حينئذ كاملة، هذا الكلام في الكفالة وأما لو تبرّع إنسان بقضاء دينه بغير الكفالة فهو صحيح بالاتفاق، وقال محمد وأبويوسف والشافعي : يصح الكفالة من الميت وإن لم يخلف مالا ولا كفيلا؛ لأن الموت لا يبري عن الدين، وإلا لما حل الأخذ من المتبرع عن الميت ولما يطالب به في الآخرة، وبه قال أحمد ومالك رحمها بل عزاه ابن قدامة إلى أكثر أهل العلم، كذا في التقرير. واستدلوا بحديث جابر الله كان رسول الله الله لا يصلي على رجل مات وعليه دين فأتي بميت، =
And as for death, it is pure incapacity by which what is of the category of obligation is lifted due to the loss of its purpose, which is performance with choice. I say: The intended meaning of it being non-existential is not that it is pure non-existence and absolute annihilation, but rather it is the separation of the soul from the body and its transfer from one abode to another, and thus the deceased is considered alive in the rulings of the Hereafter. So remember: pure incapacity: it has no aspect of ability whatsoever, and it differentiates from illness, childhood, insanity, and slavery, for in these accidents incapacity is realized but an aspect of ability remains, unlike death. Then the rulings related to the deceased are either worldly rulings or Hereafter rulings. Worldly rulings are of four categories: (1) what is of the category of obligation, like the obligation of prayer and zakah, and its ruling is removal except regarding sin; (2) what is legislated for the need of others, which has several divisions; (3) what is legislated for his own need, and its ruling is that what fulfills the need remains; (4) what is not suitable to fulfill his need, and its ruling is that it passes to the heirs. As for Hereafter rulings, their ruling is continuation. The category of obligation is an explanation of the first section of worldly rulings regarding choice. And the summary is that the purpose of obligation for the obligated person is the performance of the obligated act with choice, and with death the necessary incapacity is realized, which is not hoped to be removed, and there is no incapacity above it. For this reason we say: zakah and all other forms of worship are nullified for him, and only sin remains upon him. As for what was legislated for the need of others, if it is a right connected to a specific asset, it remains with its existence because his action therein is not intended; but if it is a debt, it does not remain by mere liability unless it is joined by wealth or what strengthens liabilities, which is the liability of a guarantor. For this reason Abu Hanifa said: suretyship (kafala) for the debt from the deceased is not valid if he did not leave wealth or a guarantor. And for this reason: i.e., due to the loss of the purpose of obligation, which is the performer’s performance with choice, it is nullified for him... etc. i.e., it drops from the deceased in worldly rulings so that it is not necessary to perform it from the estate, contrary to al-Shafi‘i, based on that the purpose is wealth not action, even if a poor person finds zakah wealth he may take the amount of zakah and it drops from his liability; whereas with us the purpose is the action which is lost by his death. And 'other forms' etc. is a conjunction on 'zakah', i.e., other forms of worship like prayer, fasting, and Hajj are nullified for him. The sin: due to his negligence in performing it while alive and healthy, and sin is from Hereafter rulings, and the deceased in Hereafter rulings is alive; if Allah wills He pardons him by His generosity and bounty, and if He wills He punishes him by His wisdom and justice. 'For the need of others': this is the second category, which has several divisions as we said: the first is what he explained by 'if it is' etc. 'Connected to an asset remains' etc., i.e., the asset like a mortgage, the right of the mortgagee is attached to it and is not invalidated by the death of the mortgagor; similarly, a lessee has a right attached to the leased property, a depositor has a right to the deposit, a buyer has a right to the purchased item—action is not intended, for the purpose in rights of people is wealth, so the right of the person remains in the asset after his death, the right-holder may take it or not without it entering the estate, and it is divided among creditors and heirs. 'To it': i.e., to the liability by the interpretation of the mentioned liability of the guarantor. The summary: if the deceased did not leave wealth or a guarantor from his lifetime, his debt does not remain in the world to be claimed from his children, but will be taken in the Hereafter. And for this reason: i.e., because there remained no liability for the debt to be joined to the guarantor’s liability, because kafala is joining liability to liability, and if there is no valid liability of the deceased, how can the guarantor’s liability be valid? Yes, if he had wealth or a guarantor during his lifetime, then kafala from him is valid because his liability at that time is complete. This discourse is about kafala; as for someone voluntarily paying his debt without kafala, it is valid by agreement. And Muhammad, Abu Yusuf, and al-Shafi‘i said: kafala from the deceased is valid even if he left no wealth or guarantor, because death does not exonerate from debt; otherwise, it would not be permissible to accept from a volunteer on behalf of the deceased, and it would not be demanded in the Hereafter. Ahmad, Malik, and Ibn Qudama attributed this to the majority of scholars. And they adduced the hadith of Jabir that the Messenger of Allah would not pray over a man who died while in debt, so a deceased was brought... =
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive Hanafi analysis of death as a celestial accident. It begins by defining death as 'pure incapacity' ('ajz khalis), meaning it completely removes any aspect of ability, unlike illness or madness where some ability remains. The author distinguishes between different categories of rulings related to the deceased: obligations of worship (like salah, zakah, hajj) are lifted in this world but the sin of negligence remains for the Hereafter; rights of others connected to specific assets (like mortgages, deposits) survive; and debts without collateral or guarantor are dropped from worldly liability unless there is wealth or a guarantor. A key dispute appears regarding kafala (suretyship) for a deceased person: Hanafi imam Abu Hanifa holds that it is invalid if the deceased left no wealth or existing guarantor, because the deceased's liability (dhimma) is no longer intact. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) alongside Shafi‘i, Malik, and Ahmad argue that death does not extinguish debt, so kafala remains valid. The text also includes a cosmological note: death is not mere annihilation but separation of soul from body, and the deceased is 'alive' for Hereafter rulings. The passage exemplifies how Hanafi usul applies the principle of 'purpose of obligation' (gharaḍ al-taklif) to deduce rulings.

Key Terms
عجز خالص ('ajz khalis): Pure incapacity, with no aspect of ability remaining, as in death.
ذمة (dhimma): Legal personality or liability that is the subject of rights and obligations.
كفالة (kafala): Suretyship: joining one's liability to another's for a debt or obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is kafala (suretyship) for a deceased person invalid if the deceased left no wealth or guarantor?
A) Because death is a punishment that erases all debts.
B) Because the deceased's dhimma is no longer intact to serve as the basis for a valid suretyship.
C) Because kafala is only valid during the lifetime of the debtor.
D) Because the beneficiary must first claim the debt from the heirs.

Key Takeaway
Death is pure incapacity that removes worldly obligations of worship and unsecured debts, but rights connected to assets and Hereafter accountability persist.

Death Nullifies Acts of Worship: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling on death nullifying ibadat with sin remaining; inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that death nullifies devotional obligations and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the purpose of zakat.

Core Idea
Death nullifies acts of worship because the action is the purpose; sin remains because the deceased is alive in the Hereafter.

This lesson examines how death, as a heavenly impediment, affects obligations in the Hanafi school. The passage states that death cancels all acts of devotion (qurub) but leaves the sin for negligence while alive. It also presents a key disagreement with the Shafi'i school over whether the purpose of zakat is the action or the wealth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heavenly impediment
Recognize that 'awāriḍ samāwiyyah are impediments like death that affect the mukallaf's capacity to perform obligations.
The passage focuses on death.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
Note that death cancels zakat and all acts of devotion (prayer, fasting, pilgrimage) in this world, but the sin for omission remains.
Sin is an eschatological concept; the deceased is considered alive in the Hereafter.
3
Identify the reason (talil)
The rationale is loss of the purpose of divine address (gharad al-taklif), which is the performance of the act by choice. Death prevents this, so the obligation drops.
This is a rational argument (qiyas).
4
Locate the dispute with Shafi'i
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the purpose of zakat is wealth (mal), so the poor may take zakat from the estate after death, discharging the obligation.
Contrast with Hanafi: purpose is action (fi'l).
5
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi does not deduct zakat from the estate; Shafi'i allows the poor to take it.
The text affirms the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القياس
عوارض سماوية
ولهذا قلنا: إنه يبطل عنه الزكاة وسائر وجوه القرب، وإنما يبقى عليه المأثم، وما شرع عليه لحاجة غيره إن كان حقاً متعلقا بالعين يبقى ببقائه؛ لأن فعله فيه غير مقصود، وإن روع المذكور أي فعل العبد كان ديناً لم يبق بمجرد الذمة حتى ينضمّ إليه مال أو ما يؤكد به الذمم وهو ذمة الكفيل.
ما شرع لحاجة الغير أي ذمة الميت
حكم الكفالة بالدين عن الميت
أي ما يؤكد به ولهذا قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: إن الكفالة بالدين عن الميت لا تصح إذا لم يخلف مالاً أو كفيلاً المفلس
ولهذا: أي لفوات غرض التكليف وهو إتيان المكلف به عن اختيار. يبطل عنه إلخ أي يسقط عن الميت في حكم الدنيا حتى لا يجب أداؤها من التركة خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله بناء على أن المقصود هو المال دون الفعل، حتى لو ظفر الفقير بمال الزكاة كان له أن يأخذ مقدار الزكاة فتسقط الزكاة عن ذمته وعندنا المقصود هو الفعل وقد فات بموته. وسائر وجوه إلخ عطف على قوله "الزكاة" أي يبطل عنه سائر وجوه القرب كالصلاة والصوم والحج. المأثم: لتقصيره في أدائه حين كان حياً صحيحاً، والإثم من أحكام الآخرة، والميت في أحكام الآخرة حي، فإن شاء الله عفا عنه بكرمه وفضله، وإن شاء عذبه بحكمته وعدله
Chapter of Qiyas: Heavenly Impediments. Because of this we say: That (death) cancels for him zakat and all other acts of devotion, and only the sin remains upon him. And what is prescribed for the need of others – if it is a right attached to a specific asset, it remains by its continuation; because his action in it is not intended. And if the action of the servant was a debt, it does not remain by mere dhimmah until wealth is joined to it or what strengthens the dhimmah, which is the dhimmah of the guarantor. What is prescribed for the need of others – i.e. the dhimmah of the deceased. Rule of suretyship for the debt of the deceased. i.e. what strengthens it. And for this Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Verily suretyship for the debt of the deceased is not valid if he has left no wealth or guarantor. The bankrupt. That is: due to the loss of the purpose of divine address, which is the doing of the obligated act by choice. 'Cancels for him' etc. meaning it drops from the deceased in worldly judgment so that its performance is not obligatory from the estate, contrary to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) based on the view that the purpose is wealth, not action, such that if a poor person finds zakat wealth, he may take the amount of zakat thus the zakat drops from his dhimma. According to us, the purpose is the action and it has been lost by death. 'And all other aspects' etc. is a coordinating conjunction on His saying 'zakat' meaning it cancels for him all other acts of devotion like prayer, fasting, and pilgrimage. 'the sin': due to his negligence in performing it while alive and sound, and sin is from the rulings of the Hereafter, and the deceased in the rulings of the Hereafter is alive; and if Allah wills He pardons him by His generosity and bounty, and if He wills He punishes him by His wisdom and justice.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that death, as a heavenly impediment ('arid samawi), nullifies certain obligations. It begins by stating the principle: acts of worship (zakat, prayer, fasting, pilgrimage) are void in this world after death, and only the sin for negligence while alive remains. The commentator explains that the purpose of divine address (gharad al-taklif) is the performance of the act by choice, which becomes impossible after death. Hence, the obligation drops in worldly terms; the estate is not required to pay zakat on behalf of the deceased. This is contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i, who considers the purpose of zakat to be wealth—so the poor can take zakat from the estate, and the debt is discharged. The Hanafi position emphasizes action over wealth. The commentator also notes that 'sin' (ma'tham) is an eschatological matter; the deceased is alive in the afterlife and may be punished or forgiven. The passage then introduces the concept of obligations for the sake of others (hajat al-ghayr), which behave differently: rights attached to assets (e.g., mortgages, leases) persist after death because the owner's action is not essential. Debts, however, require a dhimma (legal capacity) that is weakened by death; they only continue if there is property or a guarantor to strengthen it. This leads to the rule of suretyship (kafala) for the deceased's debt, where Abu Hanifa rules it invalid if no assets or guarantor are left, because the dhimma is too weak. The commentator's style is analytical, contrasting with other schools and providing the underlying rationale.

Key Terms
عوارض سماوية (awāriḍ samāwiyyah): Heavenly impediments that affect the mukallaf's capacity to perform obligations, such as death, insanity, etc.
قرب (qurub): Acts of devotion that bring one close to Allah, including prayers, fasting, pilgrimage, and zakat.
مأثم (ma'tham): The sin incurred for not performing an obligatory act while alive; it remains after death as a liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why does the obligation of zakat become null upon the death of a person?
A) Because the poor cannot claim it after death.
B) Because the purpose of zakat is the action of the mukallaf, which becomes impossible after death.
C) Because the deceased's estate is exempt from all financial obligations.
D) Because zakat is a purely spiritual act with no material aspect.

Key Takeaway
Death nullifies all devotional acts in this world because the purpose of the command is the action, but the sin for neglect remains as an eschatological liability.

Debts and Suretyship After Death: When Obligations Persist
Rule on persistence of debts and suretyship after death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rights attached to assets and debts regarding persistence after death and apply the condition for suretyship of a deceased's debt.

Core Idea
Rights attached to assets persist after death; debts require property or a guarantor to remain.

The previous lesson covered how devotional acts are nullified by death. This lesson expands on the distinction for obligations imposed for the sake of others (hajat al-ghayr). The passage explains that rights attached to specific assets continue because the owner's action is not essential, while debts on the dhimma (legal capacity) weaken with death and need backing. This leads to the Hanafi rule that suretyship for a deceased's debt is invalid unless there is property or another guarantor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of obligations
Distinguish between obligations that are purely devotional (qurub) and those prescribed for the need of others (hajat al-ghayr), such as debts or rights attached to assets.
This lesson focuses on the second category.
2
Analyze the condition for persistence
If the right is attached to a specific asset ('ayn), it remains after death because the owner's action is not required. If it is a debt (dayn), it does not persist by the mere dhimma; it needs wealth or a guarantor.
The rationale: action is not intended for asset-attached rights, but the dhimma is weak for debts.
3
Understand the implication for suretyship
Abu Hanifa ruled that suretyship (kafala) for a deceased's debt is invalid if the deceased left no wealth or prior guarantor.
Because the dhimma is too weak to support a new obligation.
4
Connect to the overall usul principle
The passage shows that the continuation of obligations after death depends on whether the purpose of the obligation requires action from the deceased or not.
This is an application of the principle of 'gharad al-taklif' discussed earlier.

Source Text

And what is prescribed for the need of others – if it is a right attached to a specific asset, it remains by its continuation; because his action in it is not intended. Key Terms
دين (dayn): A debt that is not attached to a specific asset; it is a liability on the dhimma.
ذمة (dhimmah): Legal capacity of a person to incur obligations; after death, it is weakened.
كفالة (kafālah): Suretyship; a contract where a person guarantees the debt of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is suretyship (kafala) for a deceased's debt considered valid by Abu Hanifa?
A) It is always valid if the surety is willing.
B) It is valid only if the deceased left wealth or a previous guarantor.
C) It is never valid because death nullifies all obligations.
D) It is valid if the debt was recorded in writing.

Key Takeaway
Debts and suretyship after death depend on whether the dhimma can be strengthened by assets or guarantors; otherwise, they drop.

Sila Obligations as Bequest from One-Third
Ruling on obligations of sila becoming bequest from one-third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish sila obligations from debts and explain their treatment upon death according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Sila obligations are void at death but can be fulfilled from one-third as a bequest.

In the previous discussion of heavenly impediments, we saw that certain rights are extinguished at death. This passage refines that by addressing obligations that are for the benefit of others but are not debts—like maintenance of relatives and expiations. These are called sila obligations. They become void due to the weakness of the dhimma after death, but the Lawgiver mercifully permits them to be executed from the one-third of the estate, treating them as a bequest. This rule demonstrates how Hanafi usul classifies obligations based on their nature and the capacity of the deceased's legal personality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of obligation
Read the passage and locate the word 'sila'. Understand that sila obligations are those prescribed for the need of others, not as a debt or right attached to assets.
Examples: nafaqa for relatives, kaffarat, zakat al-fitr.
2
Understand the effect of death on the dhimma
The passage states 'batala bil-mawt li-ḍuʿfi dhimmah' — the obligation becomes void because the dhimma is weak after death. Reflect on how a dead person cannot assume new voluntary obligations.
The dhimma is the legal personality capable of bearing obligations; it weakens upon death.
3
Apply the exception of the one-third bequest
Despite the voiding, the Law permits the deceased to dispose of one-third of his estate. Therefore, these obligations can be fulfilled from that portion, as if the deceased made a bequest.
This is an exemption for the benefit of the deceased and the recipients.
4
Distinguish from debts
Debts (like loans) are not void; they are paid from the entire estate even without a bequest. Sila obligations are different because they are not attached to the dhimma as a liability; they are recommended acts.
This distinction is crucial in estate distribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الحكم الذي شرع الحاجة غيره . بطريق الصلة مثل نفقة المحارم والكفارات وصدقة الفطر. بطل بالموت لضعف ذمته فيصح من الثلث لأن الشرع جوّز تصرفه في الثلث نظراً له، وقد مرّ بيانه. هذا قسم ثالث للقسم الثاني من الأقسام الأربعة، وقوله وإن كان معطوف على قوله "إن كان حقا". شرع له أي للعبد لحاجته وهذا قسم ثالث من الأقسام الأربعة حاجته ولما كان يتوهّم أن الحوائج منتهي بالموت فلا حاجة بعد الموت، أزاحه بقوله.
And if the ruling is prescribed for the need of others, by way of gift/donation (sila), such as maintenance of relatives, expiations, and sadaqat al-fitr, it becomes void by death due to the weakness of his dhimma. So it is valid from the one-third (of his estate) because the Law (shar') permitted his disposal in the one-third out of consideration for him, and its explanation has passed. This is a third division of the second division of the four divisions. And His saying 'and if' is coordinated with His saying 'if it is a right'. 'Prescribed for him' i.e. for the servant for his need, and this is a third division of the four divisions for his need. And since it might be imagined that needs cease with death and there is no need after death, he removed it by his saying.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of 'heavenly impediments' by addressing obligations that are intended for the benefit of others but are voluntary in nature (sila), such as maintenance of relatives, expiatory payments (kaffarat), and zakat al-fitr. These are obligations prescribed for the servant's own need (hajatuhu) rather than rights of others attached to assets. Upon death, these obligations are voided due to the weakness of the dhimma, but the Lawgiver has permitted that they may be fulfilled from one-third of the deceased's estate, as a form of bequest (wasiyya). The reasoning is that the deceased is allowed to dispose of up to one-third of his estate as he wishes, and this includes fulfilling such obligations. The commentator clarifies that this is the third subcase under the second major division (rights of others). The potential objection that there is no need after death is addressed by the fact that the bequest is for the sake of the deceased's spiritual benefit and the beneficiaries' need.

Key Terms
صلة (ṣilah): A gift or donation; obligations like nafaqa and kaffarat are considered sila because they are for the benefit of others but not debts.
ثلث (thuluth): One-third of the estate, up to which a person may make bequests; obligations of sila may be fulfilled from it.
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiatory payments for certain violations (e.g., broken oath); considered a financial obligation that can be willed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How are obligations like kaffarat and zakat al-fitr treated after the death of a person according to this passage?
A) They are paid from the entire estate as priority debts.
B) They become void and are not required at all.
C) They are fulfilled from one-third of the estate as a bequest.
D) They are transferred to the heirs as an obligation.

Key Takeaway
Sila obligations (nafaqa, kaffarat, sadaqat al-fitr) are not debts; they become void at death but can be fulfilled from the one-third bequest as an act of the deceased's disposal.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

Ignorance: Definition and Classification as an Acquired Hindrance
Defines ignorance and its types with reference to acquired hindrances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define simple and compound ignorance and explain why ignorance is classified as an acquired hindrance in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Ignorance is an acquired hindrance because it persists due to negligence in seeking knowledge.

In this lesson, we explore the concept of ignorance (al-jahl) as one of the acquired hindrances (al-ʿawāriḍ al-muktasaba) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. We learn the distinction between simple ignorance (absence of knowledge) and compound ignorance (false belief), and understand why even innate ignorance is considered acquired because of the obligation to seek knowledge. The passage also emphasizes the clarity of proofs for God's existence and attributes, establishing that ignorance after clear proof is not excused.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of ignorance
Simple ignorance is mere absence of knowledge; compound ignorance is firm false belief that the person thinks matches reality.
Compound ignorance is considered a defect because it resists correction.
2
Understand why ignorance is counted among acquired hindrances
Even though humans are born ignorant, the ability to learn makes persistence blameworthy; the original state is considered acquired due to negligence.
The Quranic verse (an-Nahl 78) is cited to show original ignorance.
3
Examine the Quranic proof for original ignorance
Surah an-Nahl 78 establishes that humans start without knowledge; this is not blameworthy in itself.
The verse is a dalil for the original state.
4
Appreciate the clarity of proofs for tawhid and risala
The passage cites the Bedouin's statement and verses to show that denial after such clear evidence is juhud (obdurate rejection).
This contrasts with ignorance that might be excused in worldly matters.
5
Identify the key term: juhud
Juhud is knowing the truth but rejecting it out of arrogance; it is not excused in the Hereafter.
This concept is crucial for distinguishing between excusable and non-excusable ignorance.
