الهزل أرجح لوقوعه في تعامل الناس بل وجهه بطلان الهزل برأسه في هذا القسم كما مرّ أصلنا. من وبقي الوجوه الثلاث من التسع، وهي ما إذا اتفقا على الإعراض عن الهزل، سواء كان الهزل بأصل العقد أو بقدر البدل أو بجنسه وحكم هذه الصور هو أنه يقع الطلاق ويلزم المال المسمّى بالاتفاق، أما عندهما فلبطلان الهزل برأسه، وأما عنده فلبطلان الهزل بإعراضهما عنه. ولما فرغ عن القسم الأول شرع في القسم الثاني وهو الاختبارات فقال:
وأما الإقرار إلخ. بما يحتمل الفسخ كالبيع بأن يواضعا على أن يقرا بحضور الناس ولم يكن في الواقع إقرار. بما لا يحتمله كالنكاح والطلاق بأن يواضعا على أن يقرا بالنكاح أو الطلاق بحضور الخلق ولم يكن بينهما إقرار المخبر به والإخبار وإن كان يدلّ على وجود المخبر به لكنه إذا كان بطريق الهزل فلايدل لامتناع ثبوت الشيء مع ما ينافيه. يبطله الهزل: أي كما يبطل بالهزل الإقرار كذلك يبطل به تسليم الشفعة بعد طلب الإشهاد وتوضيح المقام أن طلب الشفعة على ثلاثة أوجه الأول: طلب مواثبة وهو طلبها حالة علمه البيع على =
Matters that huzl invalidates: Contract. As for iqrar (confession), huzl invalidates it, whether the confession is of something that admits rescission or not; because huzl indicates the non-existence of the reported matter. Similarly, taslim al-shuf'a (delivery of preemption) after demand and witnessing, huzl invalidates it. ... So the wealth is immediately obligatory and the contract is binding by agreement. The disagreement is only in its extraction as the author indicates by saying: 'As for...' etc. 'Nothing occurred to them' etc. This is in three aspects: either the huzl is in the origin of the contract, or in the amount of the compensation, or in its type. They disagreed in building and turning away. This also in three aspects: first huzl in origin of contract and disagreement occurs; second in amount of compensation and disagreement occurs; third in its type and disagreement occurs in building and turning away. In those six scenarios, divorce occurs and wealth is obligatory by agreement. According to them, due to the invalidity of huzl; according to him, the author explained: 'Carrying it to seriousness' when nothing occurred to them, giving preponderance to the side of seriousness. And 'making the statement' etc. when they disagreed, so the statement of the one claiming seriousness is considered, giving preponderance to his side, as if it abrogates the first. Contrary to them, because according to them the aspect of occurrence of divorce and obligation of wealth in the two divisions (i.e., when nothing occurred to them and when they disagreed) is not carrying the contract to seriousness and considering the statement of the claimant of seriousness giving preponderance to his side; since preponderance for seriousness is not possible, rather huzl is more preponderant because it occurs in people's dealings. Rather the aspect is the invalidity of huzl itself in this division as our principle passed. And the three remaining aspects of nine remain: when they agree on turning away from huzl, whether huzl was in the origin of the contract, in the amount of compensation, or in its type. The ruling of these scenarios is that divorce occurs and the named wealth is obligatory by agreement. According to them, due to the invalidity of huzl itself; according to him, due to the invalidity of huzl by their turning away from it. When the first division was finished, he began the second division, which is al-ikhtibarat (tests), saying: 'As for iqrar, etc.' Including what admits rescission like sale, by them agreeing to confess in front of people while there was no actual confession. Including what does not admit rescission like marriage and divorce, by them agreeing to confess to marriage or divorce in front of the public while there was no confession between them. The reported matter and the report, although it indicates the existence of the reported matter, but when it is done by way of huzl, it does not indicate, due to the impossibility of establishing a thing with what contradicts it. Huzl invalidates it: just as huzl invalidates iqrar, similarly it invalidates taslim al-shuf'a after demand and witnessing. Clarification: demand of shuf'a has three types: first, demand of muwathaba, which is demanding it at the time of his knowledge of the sale on equal footing =
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the effect of huzl (jest) by listing specific acts that are invalidated by huzl. The author first states that huzl invalidates iqrar (confession), whether the subject matter of the confession admits rescission (e.g., sale) or not (e.g., marriage, divorce). The reason is that huzl indicates the non-existence of the reported fact; a confession made in jest contradicts the truthfulness required. Similarly, huzl invalidates taslim al-shuf'a (delivery of preemption) after demand and witnessing. The text then delves into a more detailed analysis of the earlier discussion on khul', reviewing the six scenarios where there is agreement on the occurrence of divorce and obligation of wealth. It explains the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two companions regarding the preponderance of seriousness or jest when the parties disagree. According to Abu Hanifa, when there is no recollection of jest, the contract is carried to seriousness; if they disagree, the word of the one claiming seriousness is accepted, as if his statement abrogates the initial jest. The companions argue that jest is more preponderant in people's dealings, so the contract is not carried to seriousness; rather, huzl is inherently invalid. The passage then moves to the second division (al-ikhtibarat) focusing on iqrar, providing examples: people agreeing to falsely confess in jest. The core principle is that huzl prevents the report from indicating the truth of the reported matter. The passage ends by starting to explain the types of shuf'a demand, beginning with muwathaba (immediate demand). This illustrates how Hanafi jurists apply the principle of huzl consistently across different areas of law: contracts, confessions, and preemption.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrar): A legal confession or admission of a right or fact, which is normally binding.
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): The right of preemption in property sales, allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase the sold share.
طلب المواثبة (talab al-muwathaba): Immediate demand of preemption upon knowledge of the sale.
هزل (huzl): Jest, joking, or speech not intended to be taken seriously.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because iqrar is a form of contract.
B) Because huzl indicates the non-existence of the reported matter.
C) Because the parties intended to deceive.
D) Because a confession cannot be conditional.

Key Takeaway
Huzl invalidates iqrar and taslim al-shuf'a because jest contradicts the truthfulness required for a report, as established by the rational evidence that huzl indicates the non-existence of the reported matter.

Effect of Jest on Contracts: Disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Examination of jest's effect on contracts and the intra-madhhab dispute over legal causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on jest in contracts and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the legal causation of the contract's validity.

Core Idea
Jest in contracts does not void them; the contract is interpreted as serious, but Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on why.

This lesson explores a key usul al-fiqh dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the effect of jest (hazl) on contractual acts. The passage presents the ruling of the matn and the commentary's detailed analysis of nine scenarios, focusing on the differing legal reasoning between Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to trace the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) from differing usul principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling of the matn
The matn states that when both parties agree no jest occurred or they differ, the contract is interpreted as serious (jidd) and the word of the one claiming seriousness is taken. This is Abu Hanifa's view.
The ruling is that the contract is valid and binding.
2
Distinguish the three scenarios of jest
The commentary breaks down nine scenarios: jest in the original contract, in the amount, or in kind; combined with both parties aware, both unaware, or differing.
In six of these, both schools agree divorce and payment are due.
3
Understand the disagreement in ta'lil (legal reasoning)
Abu Hanifa argues that the contract is carried upon seriousness as a default, preferring the side of jidd. The Sahibayn argue that jest is inherently void (bāṭil), so the contract remains valid without needing to assume seriousness.
The dispute is not about the outcome but the underlying usul basis.
4
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference arises when one party claims jest and the other claims seriousness. Abu Hanifa takes the word of the one claiming seriousness; the Sahibayn would not give preference and would treat the contract as valid regardless.
This shows how usul principles affect case outcomes.

Chapter of Analogy: Acquired impediments. As for if they agree that nothing was present [of jest] or they differ, it is attributed to seriousness and the statement of the claimant is upheld in the view of Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two [companions]. Matters that jest renders void: Contract. Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary discusses the effect of jest (hazl) on contractual acts. It opens by presenting a ruling from the matn: when two parties agree that no jest occurred or they differ, the contract is interpreted as serious (jidd), and the word of the one claiming seriousness is taken, according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn). The text then states that jest invalidates acknowledgment, preemption, and freeing of debt, but focuses here on contracts. The commentary details nine scenarios combining three locations of jest (original contract, amount of substitute, type of substitute) and three situations (both aware, both unaware, or differing). In six of these, both schools agree that divorce occurs and payment is due, but they differ on the reasoning: Abu Hanifa attributes the contract to seriousness by default, while the Sahibayn argue that jest itself is invalid and thus the contract stands as if no jest occurred. The remaining three scenarios involve both parties abandoning the jest, where again both schools agree on the outcome but differ on the basis. The author then transitions to the second section on tests (ikhtibar).

Key Terms
هزل (hazl): The act of joking or jesting in a legal transaction, which can affect its validity.
جد (jidd): Seriousness, the opposite of jest; contracts are interpreted as serious by default.
بطلان (buṭlān): Voidness; the state of being null and void without legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Jest in contracts does not automatically invalidate them; the Hanafi position balances seriousness as the default, while the Sahibayn treat jest as inherently void, leading to the same outcome but through different usul reasoning.

Jest Invalidating Legal Acts: Acknowledgment, Preemption, and Freeing of Debt
Expansion of the rule that jest invalidates acts requiring satisfaction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that jest (hazl) invalidates legal acts requiring satisfaction (rida) and distinguish the temporal conditions for preemption surrender.

Core Idea
Jest negates satisfaction, voiding acts like acknowledgment, preemption surrender, and freeing debt - same as khiyar al-shart.

This passage expands the well-known rule that jest invalidates contracts to other legal acts: acknowledgment, preemption surrender, and freeing of debt. Understanding the underlying usul principle—that these acts require genuine satisfaction (rida) which jest contradicts—allows you to apply the rule consistently across different fiqh chapters. The commentary draws an explicit analogy with khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) to explain the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The matn states that jest invalidates acknowledgment (iqrar) regardless of whether the acknowledged matter is rescindable (like sale) or not (like marriage/divorce). This is because jest contradicts the inherent indication of truth in an acknowledgment.
Acknowledgment is a khabar (informative statement); jest makes it ineffective.
2
Understand the reasoning through khiyar al-shart analogy
The commentator explains that jest is like khiyar al-shart in that both prevent the satisfaction (rida) required for a valid surrender of preemption. If preemption is surrendered after the two demands (immediate and witnessing) with a jest, the surrender is void and preemption remains.
Contrast: if surrendered jestingly before the immediate demand, it is like silence and voids preemption entirely.
3
Extend the principle to freeing of debt
Freeing of debt (ibra) involves tamlik (transfer of ownership). Tamlik is affected by jest because ownership can be rejected, similar to khiyar al-shart. Therefore, jest invalidates ibra, and the debt remains.
The reasoning is analogical (qiyas): shared illa is prevention of satisfaction.
4
Apply the usul principle to new cases
The common thread: any legal act that depends on mutual consent or genuine intention is void if performed jestingly, unless a specific textual exception exists. Hanafi school applies this broadly across muamalat.
This is derived from the general rule that 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (acts are judged by intentions).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for acknowledgment, etc.: concerning what can be rescinded like sale, such as when they agree that they should acknowledge in front of people while in reality there is no acknowledgment; and concerning what cannot be rescinded like marriage and divorce, such as when they agree to acknowledge marriage or divorce in front of people while there was no acknowledgment between them. The information, although it indicates the existence of the informed thing, but if it is by way of jest, it does not indicate because it is impossible to establish a thing with what contradicts it. Jest renders it void: meaning just as jest renders acknowledgment void, likewise jest renders void the surrender of preemption after demand and witnessing. Clarification: the demand of preemption has three aspects: First: immediate demand, which is demanding it at the moment of knowing the sale, so that it becomes void if not demanded immediately; Second: demand of witnessing and confirmation, by standing after immediate demand and testifying to the seller, the buyer, or at the property, and by this demand the preemption is established and is not voided by delay in demanding possession; Third: demand of litigation and ownership. Knowing this, if the preemptor surrenders the preemption by jest after the two demands, his surrender is void and his preemption remains. Such as option of stipulation: even if he surrenders it after the two demands with the condition of option for three days, his surrender is void and his preemption remains, because it depends on satisfaction with the ruling, and option of stipulation prevents satisfaction, so the surrender is void due to lack of satisfaction. Jest is like option of stipulation in preventing satisfaction, so it is also void by jest due to lack of satisfaction. This is if he surrenders by jest after immediate demand and witnessing; but if he surrenders by jest before immediate demand, the preemption is void, because surrendering by jest is like remaining silent regarding immediate demand, and preemption is void by silence. Freeing of debt by way of jest is rendered void by jest; even if he frees a debtor by jest, the debt remains as it was, because in freeing there is a meaning of making ownership, and ownership is returned by rejection, so jest affects it like option of stipulation. When he finished the second section, he began the section concerning belief, saying:
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on jest, focusing on three specific acts: acknowledgment (iqrar), surrender of preemption (taslim al-shuf‘a), and freeing of debt (ibra al-gharim). The matn states that jest invalidates acknowledgment whether it concerns something that can be rescinded (like sale) or cannot (like marriage/divorce), because jest contradicts the informational content. The commentary explains that the parties might agree to simulate an acknowledgment without real intent, and jest negates the inherent indication of truth. The same rule applies to the surrender of preemption after the two demands (immediate and witnessing): if done jokingly, it is invalid and preemption remains. The analogy is made with option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart), which also nullifies surrender because satisfaction (rida) is absent. However, if surrendering jestingly before the immediate demand, the preemption is void as if by silence. As for freeing debt, jest also invalidates it, because freeing involves transfer of ownership, and such transfer is vulnerable to rejection, so jest—like option of stipulation—prevents valid transfer. The section ends by transitioning to the next topic concerning matters of belief (i‘tiqad).

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): An acknowledgment of a right or debt, which is invalid if done in jest.
شفعة (shuf‘a): Preemption right; jest invalidates its surrender after the two demands.
إبراء (ibra): Freeing a debtor from debt; jest invalidates it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The preemption becomes optional
D) The surrender is considered as if it were a sale

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, jest (hazl) invalidates legal acts requiring satisfaction, and this principle extends via analogy to acts like preemption surrender and freeing of debt.

Jest in Utterances of Faith: The Hanafi Principle of Binding Effect
usul rule with dalil naqli

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that jest does not affect utterances related to faith and derive the rulings for conversion and apostasy using the Quranic evidence from At-Tawbah 9:65-66.

Core Idea
Jest in faith matters is binding; utterance overrides intent.

This passage from Muntakhab al-Husami explores a critical usul principle: words of faith are taken at face value regardless of the speaker's jesting intent. Whether a disbeliever utters the shahada in jest or a Muslim utters kufr in jest, the ruling follows the literal meaning of the words, not the internal state. We will unpack the derivation (istinbat) of this rule from the Quran and examine its implications for the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two cases: (1) a disbeliever jestingly says the shahada, (2) a Muslim jestingly says a word of disbelief. Note that in both, the speaker does not intend the meaning.
The rulings are opposite: entering and leaving Islam.
2
Extract the governing usul principle
The principle is: 'Jest does not affect the legal effect of an utterance of faith; the utterance is insha' (initiation) and takes effect immediately.' This is derived from the analogy to coercion (mukrah) and from the Quranic verse.
In Hanafi usul, the literal meaning (haqiqah) prevails over figurative intent in such declarations.
3
Locate the dalil and analyse its wajh al-dalalah
The Quranic verse (At-Tawbah 9:65-66) states that the hypocrites who mocked were declared disbelievers. The operative phrase is 'you have disbelieved after your belief.' The verse proves that jesting with disbelief constitutes apostasy because Allah judges by the outward utterance, not the inner mockery.
The dalil is qat'i al-thubut (Quran) and qat'i al-dalalah (clear indication).
4
Determine the epistemological weight
The ruling is supported by consensus (ijma') and the explicit text. In the Hanafi hierarchy, this places the rule at the level of qat'i (certain) and thus not open to istihsan.
The passage states 'by consensus and textual evidence'.
5
Apply the rule to hypothetical scenarios
Test the principle: if a person says 'There is no god but Allah' while laughing, he becomes Muslim instantly. If a Muslim says 'I am a disbeliever' as a joke, he becomes an apostate. The external utterance is decisive.
This contrasts with the Shafi'i view where intention matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ من القسم الثاني شرع في القسم وهو ما يتعلق بالاعتقاد فقال: وأما الكافر إذا تكلّم بكلمة الإسلام وتبرأ عن دينه هازلاً يجب أن يحكم بإيمانه كالمكره؛ لأنه بمنزلة إنشاء لا يحتمل حكم الرد والتراخي. أي الإيمان الفور حتى يبطل الشفعة لعدم هذا الطلب، ... ولما أعرض المصنف عن أحكام الردة فعلينا أن نذكرها، وهي أنه إذا تكلم بكلمة الكفر هازلاً غير قاصد معناها يصير مرتدا بنفس الهزل؛ لأنه باشرها باختيارها لا بما هزل به وهو مدلوله؛ لأنه غير معتقد لذلك. فالحاصل أنه كافراً يصير بالتكلم بكلمة الكفر هازلاً بالإجماع والنص، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَئِن سَأَلْتَهُمْ لَيَقُولُنَّ إِنَّمَا كُنَّا نَحُوضُ وَنَلْعَبُ قُلْ أَبِاللَّهِ وَآيَاتِهِ وَرَسُولِهِ كُنتُمْ تَسْتَهْزِئُونَ لا تَعتذِرُوا قَد كَفَرْتُم بعد إيمانكم﴾ (التوبة:٦٥)، حكم بكفرهم بذلك الاستهزاء، ولما فرغ عن بحث الهزل شرع في بحث السفه فقال: وأما السفه إلخ.
When he finished the second section, he began the section concerning belief, saying: As for the disbeliever, if he utters the word of Islam and disavows his religion jestingly, it is obligatory to judge him as a believer, like the one coerced; because it is like an initiation that does not bear the ruling of return and delay. i.e., faith immediately... Then when the author omitted the rulings on apostasy, it is upon us to mention them: That if he utters a word of disbelief jestingly without intending its meaning, he becomes an apostate by the very jest; because he performed it by his choice, not by what he jested with, which is its meaning, since he does not believe that. The conclusion: one becomes a disbeliever by uttering a word of disbelief jestingly by consensus and textual evidence, which is His saying: {And if you ask them, they will surely say, 'We were only conversing and playing.' Say, 'Is it Allah and His verses and His Messenger that you were mocking?' Make no excuse; you have disbelieved after your belief.} (At-Tawbah 9:65-66). He judged their disbelief by that mockery. When he finished the discussion on jest, he began the discussion on foolishness (safah), saying: As for foolishness, etc.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This final passage addresses the effect of jest on matters of faith. It begins by presenting the matn: if a disbeliever utters the testimony of Islam (shahada) and disavows his religion in jest, he is judged as a Muslim in worldly matters, analogous to one coerced into Islam. The reason is that these words are an initiation (insha) that cannot be undone or delayed; they take effect immediately. The author then notes that the matn omitted the rules on apostasy (ridda), so the commentator explains: if a Muslim utters a word of disbelief jestingly without intending its meaning, he becomes an apostate by the mere jest, because he chose to utter the words, though he does not believe them. This is supported by a Quranic verse (At-Tawbah 9:65-66) where the hypocrites mocked and were declared disbelievers. Thus, jest in faith-related statements is effective: for a disbeliever, it brings him into Islam; for a Muslim, it takes him out. The passage ends the section on jest and moves to discuss foolishness (safah).

Key Terms
هزل (hazl): Jest; in matters of faith, it is effective: the utterance is taken literally despite the intention to joke.
ردة (ridda): Apostasy from Islam; committed by jestingly uttering a word of disbelief.
مكره (mukrah): One coerced; analogy to the coerced convert who is judged Muslim despite lack of consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a Muslim jokingly says a word of disbelief without intending its meaning?
A) He does not become an apostate because he did not intend it
B) He becomes an apostate by consensus and Quranic evidence
C) He is considered a hypocrite but not an apostate
D) He must repeat the shahada to remain Muslim

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, jest does not affect the legal ruling of faith utterances; the outward word is binding.

Safah: Definition and Effect on Legal Capacity
Definition and legal analysis of safah and its effect on capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define safah in Hanafi usul, distinguish it from celestial defects, and explain the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on its effect on legal capacity and guardianship.

Core Idea
Safah is an acquired sin, not a celestial defect; Abu Hanifa holds it does not strip legal capacity.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, legal capacity (ahliyya) is affected by certain attributes such as insanity or imbecility. One disputed attribute is safah (prodigality). This passage defines safah and presents the positions of the founding imams on whether it necessitates legal restriction (hajr). The discussion reveals a key methodological principle: the conditions under which qiyas (analogy) is valid.

Guided Path
1
Define safah linguistically and technically
Read the two definitions given: linguistic 'lightness' and technical 'wasting wealth contrary to intellect and law'. Note which definition is relevant to the fiqh discussion.
The technical definition excludes sins like zina; the discussion focuses on financial prodigality.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling on safah
According to Abu Hanifa, safah does not remove legal capacity (ahliyya al-ada'), does not prevent any Sharia rulings, and does not necessitate hajr. Identify the key reason: safah is an acquired attribute, not celestial.
The person retains intellect and acts by choice, so capacity remains intact.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position and its scope
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ: they hold that safah requires hajr for transactions that jest would invalidate (e.g., sale, lease) but not for acts like marriage or emancipation.
The distinction depends on whether the act is voidable by jest (yubtiluhu al-hazl).
4
Analyze the proofs for each position
The Sahibayn cite Quran 2:282 and the public burden argument. Abu Hanifa responds that the verse is specific to debt dictation and that safah is a sin, not a valid cause for pity. Also note the objection and rebuttal regarding the prevention of wealth at age 25.
The qiyas argument from prevention fails because the prevention ruling is ta'abbudi (worshipped) and not ma'qul al-ma'na.
5
Apply the dispute to a concrete example
Consider a safih selling his house. According to Abu Hanifa the sale is valid; according to the Sahibayn it is invalid without guardian consent. This illustrates the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
This difference affects real transactions and guardianship decisions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السفه فلايخل بالأهلية، ولا يمنع شيئاً من أحكام الشرع، ولا يوجب الحجر عند أبي حنيفة له، وكذا عند غيره فيما لا يبطله الهزل؛ لأنه مكابرة العقل بغلبة لا يوجب الحجر أي السفه الهوى، فلم يكن سبباً للنظر، ومنع المال عن السفيه المبذر في أول البلوغ ثبت بالنص، أما عقوبة عليه أو غير معقول المعنى فلا يحتمل المقايسة. السفه فهو في اللغة الخفة وفي الشرع تبذير المال على خلاف مقتضى العقل والشرع، وقيل: إنه خفة تعتري الإنسان فتحمله على العمل، بخلاف موجب الشرع والعقل مع قيام العقل حقيقةً، فالسفيه يعمل باختياره على خلاف مقتضى العقل والشرع مع بقاء العقل، فيكون السفه من العوارض المكتسبة ولا يكون سماوياً، والمعنى الأخير وإن كان مناسبا للمعنى اللغوي ولكنه يشمل ارتكاب المحرمات كالزنا وشرب الخمر وهو إن كان سفها ولكنه غير مبحوث في هذا المقام، والمعنى الأول يناسب المقام وإن لم يناسب المعنى اللغوي. بالأهلية مطلقاً، لا أهلية الخطاب ولا أهلية الوجوب لوجود العقل. ولا يمنع شيئاً إلخ من الوجوب له وعليه، فيكون مطالباً بالأحكام كلها. ولا يوجب الحجر إلخ سواء كان في تصرف لا يبطله الهزل كالعتاق والنكاح، أو في تصرف يبطله الهزل كالبيع والإجارة؛ لأنه حرّ مخاطب بالتكاليف، فله التصرف في ماله مثل الرشيد، والجامع الحرية وأهلية الخطاب غيره أي عند أبي يوسف ومحمد. فيما لا يبطله الهزل: وأما في ما يبطله الهزل يحجر عليه نظراً له لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنْ كَانَ الَّذِي عَلَيْهِ الْحَقُّ سَفِيهاً أَوْ ضَعِيفاً أَوْ لَا يَسْتَطِيعُ أَنْ يُمِلَّ هُوَ فَلْيُمْلِلْ وَلِيُّهُ بِالْعَدْلِ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٨٢) نص على إثبات الولاية على السفيه، ولأنه بعد تبذير ماله يحتاج لنفقته إلى بيت المال، فيكون كلا على المسلمين. سبباً للنظر : هذا دليل على أن السفه لا يصلح أن يكون سبباً للحجر عليه نظرا له، وحاصله أن السفه ليس أمراً سماوياً، كالجنون والعته حتى يرحم عليه وينظر له بل هو معصية لمكابرة العقل واتباع الهوى مع العلم بقبحه وفساد عاقبته، فلا يصلح سبباً للنظر له فلا يحجر عليه نظراً له. ومنع المال إلخ: دفع لما يرد على أبي حنيفة بالله وهو أنه يقول بمنع المال عن السفيه أول بلوغه خمسة وعشرين سنة إذا كان مبذراً نظر إليه لحفظ ماله عن التلف، فهذا القول يدلّ على أن السفه سبب للنظر له، فإذا صار سبباً للنظر بمنع ماله صار سببا للحجر عليه في تصرفاته أيضا؛ لأن المنع والحجر في حفظ ماله سواء، فيقاس الحجر على المنع تقرير الجواب أن من شروط القياس كون المقيس عليه معقول المعنى وكونه غير عقوبة، وذلك الشرط منتف ههنا؛ لأن المقيس عليه وهو المنع الذي ثبت بالنص:
As for foolishness (safah), it does not remove legal capacity, nor does it prevent any of the rulings of the Sharia, nor does it necessitate legal restriction (hajr) according to Abu Hanifa. Similarly, according to others, regarding that which jest does not invalidate; because it is a contradiction of the intellect by desire that does not necessitate restriction - i.e., safah is desire, so it is not a cause for looking out (for his benefit). The prevention of property from the prodigal at the beginning of maturity is established by text, but either as a punishment or as something not rationally understandable, it cannot be measured by analogy. Safah linguistically is lightness, and in Sharia is wasting wealth contrary to the dictates of intellect and law. It is said: it is a lightness that afflicts a person and drives him to act contrary to what the law and intellect require, while intellect actually remains. So the fool acts by his own choice against reason and law while retaining intellect, thus safah is an acquired incidental cause, not celestial. The latter meaning, although fitting the linguistic meaning, includes committing forbidden acts like adultery and drinking wine, which, although foolishness, is not discussed here. The first meaning suits the context even if it does not fit the linguistic meaning. (Safah does not remove) capacity absolutely, neither capacity of address nor capacity of obligation because intellect exists. Nor does it prevent anything of the obligation for him and upon him, so he is required (to fulfill) all rulings. Nor does it necessitate restriction etc., whether in an act that jest does not invalidate like emancipation and marriage, or in an act that jest does invalidate like sale and lease; because he is free and addressed by obligations, so he may dispose of his property like a mature person. The common factor is freedom and capacity of address. Others (i.e., according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): regarding that which jest does not invalidate: but regarding that which jest does invalidate, he is restricted for his own benefit due to His saying: "But if the one under obligation is foolish or weak or unable to dictate himself, then let his guardian dictate justly" (Al-Baqarah 2:282). This text establishes guardianship over the fool, and because after wasting his wealth he needs his expenses from the public treasury, becoming a burden on Muslims. 'A cause for looking out': this is evidence that safah is not suitable as a cause for restriction for his own benefit. The summary is that safah is not a celestial matter like insanity or imbecility such that one pities him and looks out for him; rather it is a sin by opposing intellect and following desire while knowing its ugliness and evil outcome, so it is not suitable as a cause for looking out for him, so he is not restricted for his own benefit. 'The prevention of wealth etc.': this is a refutation of what is objected to Abu Hanifa, namely that he says prevention of wealth from the fool at the beginning of his maturity at twenty-five years if he is a waster, looking out for him to preserve his wealth from loss. This statement indicates that safah is a cause for looking out for him, so if it becomes a cause for looking out by preventing his wealth, it becomes a cause for restriction in his transactions as well, because prevention and restriction are the same in preserving his wealth. So restriction is analogized to prevention. The answer is that one of the conditions of analogy is that the object of comparison (al-maqis 'alayh) be rationally understandable and not a punishment, and that condition is absent here because the object of comparison, which is prevention established by text...
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition and ruling on safah (foolishness/prodigality) and its effect on legal capacity (ahliyya). The author presents the Hanafi position, particularly that of Imam Abu Hanifa, who holds that safah does not affect a person's legal capacity, nor does it necessitate legal restriction (hajr) on his property. This is because safah is an acquired defect, not a celestial one like insanity (junun) or imbecility ('atah). The person retains intellect and acts by choice, so he is considered fully capable. However, the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they hold that a safih should be restricted in transactions that would be invalidated by jest, such as sale and lease, to protect his property. They cite the Quranic verse (2:282) which establishes guardianship over the fool. The passage also addresses an objection: if Abu Hanifa allows preventing the safih from his wealth at the age of 25 (if he is a prodigal) as a protective measure, why does he not allow restriction? The answer is that prevention of wealth is a specific text-based ruling (ta'abbudi) and is not subject to analogy because its underlying rationale is not rationally understandable or is a punishment. Thus, analogizing restriction to prevention is invalid because it would require that the original ruling be ma'qul al-ma'na (rationally understandable) and not a punishment, conditions that are absent here.

Key Terms
سفه (safah): Foolishness or prodigality; legally, wasting wealth contrary to reason and law while retaining intellect.
حجر (hajr): Legal restriction preventing a person from disposing of his property; guardianship.
ولاية (wilaya): Guardianship or authority over another's affairs, here specifically over property.
عقل (aql): Intellect; the faculty that distinguishes humans and is a condition for legal capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does safah not necessitate legal restriction?
A) Because the person lacks intellect.
B) Because it is a celestial defect.
C) Because it is an acquired sin and the person retains intellect.
D) Because the Quran does not mention safah.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa), safah (prodigality) does not remove legal capacity or necessitate restriction because it is an acquired attribute, not a celestial defect, and the person retains intellect.

Types of Coercion and Their Legal Effects
Defines two types of ikrah and their impact on choice and rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define complete and deficient coercion and apply the Hanafi principle that coercion does not remove legal capacity.

Core Idea
Coercion negates satisfaction but the coerced person remains legally responsible.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi classification of coercion (ikrah). The passage defines complete coercion (ikrah kamil) which eliminates choice, and deficient coercion (ikrah qasir) which only negates consent. It also clarifies that coercion in all its forms does not remove legal capacity (ahliyyah), so the coerced person remains subject to divine address. Understanding this typology is essential for correctly determining the ruling on actions performed under duress.

Guided Path
1
Classify the types of coercion
Identify ikrah kamil (complete) and ikrah qasir (deficient). Note their effects: kamil spoils choice (ikhtiyar) and necessitates compulsion (ilja'), while qasir only negates satisfaction (rida). Also recognize a third type where neither is negated (threat to family).
Complete coercion involves threat of death or severe bodily harm.
2
Understand the principle of remaining capacity
The passage states: 'coercion in its entirety does not negate legal capacity, nor does it cause the removal of address.' The coerced person is tested (mubtala) and remains addressed by the Lawgiver. This means he is still accountable.
The Quranic verse (6:119) is cited to support that compulsion creates permissibility, but not exemption from address.
3
Distinguish legal outcomes
Identify the four possible rulings for a coerced act: obligation (fard), prohibition (haram), permissibility (ibaha), and license (rukhsa). Examples: eating carrion under death threat becomes obligatory; killing a Muslim remains prohibited and one should endure death; breaking fast becomes permissible; uttering disbelief becomes a license if heart is firm.
The difference between ibaha and rukhsas: in ibaha, the prohibition is removed; in rukhsas, it remains but the sin is lifted.
4
Apply the no-license rule for killing and wounding
The passage explicitly states 'no license in killing and wounding for the coerced person.' Even under threat of death, one may not kill or wound another because the victim's sanctity is equal to one's own. The coercion is treated as non-existent due to conflict of equal sanctities.
This is a key exception: coercion does not permit taking an innocent life.

باب القياس
أنواع الإكراه
لكنه لما كان من الأمور المختارة ولم يكن موجباً ضرورة لازمة، قيل: إنه إذا أصبح صائماً وهو مسافر، أو مقيم فسافر لايباح له الفطر بخلاف المريض، ولو أفطر كان قيام السفر المبيح شبهة في إيجاب الكفارة، ولو أفطر ثم سافر لا يسقط عنه الكفارة بخلاف ما إذا مرض لما قلنا.
المقيم في موضع الإقامة
[أنواع الإكراه]
وأما الإكراه فنوعان كامل يفسد الاختيار ويوجب الإلجاء، وقاصر يعدم الرضاء ولايوجب الإلجاء،
النوع الأول إكراه ويعدم الرضاء ولا يفسد الاختيار
الأمور المختارة: أي من الأمور التي وجودها باختيار الفاعل، ومن هنا ظهر التفرقة بين السفر والمرض؛ لأن المرض ليس وجوده باختيار المريض بل هو أمر مساوي.
ضرورة لازمةً: يعني بعد ما تحقق السفر لا يوجب ضرورةً تدعو إلى الإفطار بحيث لا يمكن دفعها؛ إذ المسافر يقدر على الصوم من غير أن يلحقه آفة في بدنه لايباح له الفطر: لأنه قد تقرر الوجوب عليه بالشروع، ولا ضرورة له تدعو إلى الإفطار بخلاف المريض: إذا تكلّف للصوم ثم أراد أن يفطر حلّ له ذلك، وكذا إذا مرض بعد ما أصبح صائماً حلّ له ذلك؛ لأن المرض أمر سماوي لا اختيار للعبد فيه. ولو أفطر المسافر في الصورتين *المذكورتين في إيجاب الكفارة: فلا تجب الكفارة عليه؛ لأنها إنما تحب بالإفطار عن صوم واجب من غير اقتران الشبهة. لا يسقط إلخ لعدم السفر المبيح الذي كان شبهة في إيجاب الكفارة. إذا مرض بعد أن أفطر في حالة الصحة حيث تسقط عنه الكفارة لما قلنا من أن المرض أمر سماوي لا اختيار للعبد فيه، فكأنه أفطر في حال المرض. لما فرغ من القسم الأول من العوارض المكتسبة شرع في القسم الثاني وهو ما يقع عليه من غيره فقال: وأما الإكراه : وهو أن يجبر القادر غيره على أمر لايريده لولا الخوف منه بالوعيد على إيقاع ما يوعد به ويوجب الإلجاء: أي يضطر المكره إلى أن يفعل ما أمره به بما يخاف على نفسه أو عضو من أعضائه بأن يقول المكره : افعل هذا وإلا لأقتلنك، أو لأقطعن يدك، فحينئذ يفسد اختياره وينعدم رضاه ألبتة، والنوع الثاني إكراه الإلجاء: وهو الإكراه بضرب لا يخاف على نفسه التلف، =
الصور الصورة الأولى هي أن يصوم المسافر ثم يفطر، والثانية: أن يصوم المقيم ثم يسافر ثم يفطر، فلا كفارة في هاتين الصورتين. نعم تحب الكفارة في الصورة الثالثة، أن يصوم المقيم ثم يفطر قبل أن يسافر. وهي

بالفتح
والإكراه بجملته لا ينافي أهلية، ولا يوجب وضع الخطاب بحال؛ لأن المكره مبتلى، أي بجميع الأقسام المذكورة والابتلاء يحقق الخطاب. ألا ترى أنه متردّد بين فرض وحظر وإباحة ورخصة، ويأثم المكره في إتيان ما أكره عليه
فيه مرةً ويؤجر أخرى، فلا رخصة في القتل والجرح للمكره
= أو بقيد، أو بحبس مدة مديدة، ففي هذا النوع يبقى الاختيار ولا لكن لا يرضى بما يفعل، وبقي قسم ثالث وهو ما لا يعدم الرضاء ولا يفسد الاختيار بأن يهم بحبس أبيه أو ابنه أو زوجته أو نحوه، ففي هذا القسم الرضاء والاختيار كلاهما باق أهلية لا أهلية الوجوب ولا أهلية الأداء.

وضع الخطاب: أي سقوطه عن المكره. بحال سواء كان الإكراه ملجئاً أو لا، لوجود الذمة والعقل الذي عليه مدار الخطاب. مبتلى في حالة الإكراه كما أنه مبتلى في حالة الاختيار. يحقق الخطاب: لأنه لا يثبت بدونه، ثم استدلّ على ثبوت الابتلاء وتحقق الخطاب به بين :فرض كما إذا أكره على أكل الميتة بالقتل فإنه حينئذ يفرض عليه أكلها، ولو صبر حتى مات يأثم؛ لأن أكلها كان مباحاً له؛ لأنه تعالى قال: ﴿إِلَّا مَا اضْطُرِرْتُمْ إِلَيْهِ (الأنعام: ۱۱۹)، فثبت الإباحة بالاستثناء، ومن أكره على مباح يفترض عليه فعله. وحظر أي ممنوع في حالة الإكراه أيضا كما إذا أكره على قتل مسلم بغير حق، فإن صبر حتى مات يؤجر، وإباحة كما أكره على الإفطار في نهار رمضان فحينئذ يباح له الفطر ورخصة كما إذا أكره على إجراء كلمة الكفر على لسانه، فحينئذ يرخص له ذلك بشرط أن يكون التصديق في قلبه والفرق بين الرخصة والإباحة هو في المباح ترتفع الحرمة، وفي الرخصة لا ترتفع الحرمة بل يرفع الإثم فقط، والأولى عدم ذكر الإباحة؛ لأنها إن كان مع الإثم في الصبر فهي الفرض، وإلا فهي الرخصة، فالحاصل أنها داخل في الفرض أو الرخصة. ويأثم فيه أي في الإقدام على ما أكره عليه إذا كان حراماً كقتل المسلم بغير حق. أخرى: في الإقدام على ما أكره عليه إذا كان فرضا كأكل الميتة إذا أكره عليه بالقتل، وكل من الأجر والإثم إنما يكون بعد تعلق الخطاب، فثبت أن المكره مخاطب مبتلى. ثم أشار إلى أمثلة الفرض والحظر والرخصة والإباحة بقوله: فلا رخصة إلخ. في القتل: أي قتل غيره إذا خاف على نفسه الهلاك؛ لأنهما في استحقاق العصمة سواء، فلايكون له صيانة نفسه بإتلاف غيره، فصار الإكراه في حكم العدم لتعارض الحرمتين مع عدم المرجح. والجرح وكذا لا رخصة له في جرح غيره إذا قيل له: اقطع يد فلان وإلا أقتلك، حتى لو قطع كان آثما؛ لأن أطراف المؤمن في الحرمة مثل حرمة نفسه في حق الغير، فتحقق التعارض بين حرمة نفسه وحرمة جرح غيره، فلم يترجح =
Chapter of Qiyas
Types of Ikrah
But since it was from matters of choice and did not necessitate a necessary exigency, it was said: If one becomes a fasting person while he is a traveler, or a resident then travels, breaking the fast is not permissible for him, unlike the sick person. If he broke the fast, the existence of travel that permits breaking would be a doubt in the obligation of expiation. If he broke the fast then traveled, the expiation is not waived for him, unlike when he falls ill, due to what we said.
The resident in the place of residence
[Types of Ikrah]
As for coercion, it is of two types: complete, which spoils choice and necessitates compulsion; and deficient, which negates satisfaction but does not necessitate compulsion.
The first type is coercion that negates satisfaction and does not spoil choice.
Matters of choice: meaning from matters whose existence is by the choice of the doer. From here appears the distinction between travel and illness; because illness does not exist by the choice of the patient, but it is an involuntary matter.
Necessary exigency: meaning after travel is realized, it does not necessitate an exigency that calls for breaking the fast such that it cannot be repelled; since the traveler can fast without harm to his body, breaking is not permissible for him: because the obligation is established upon him by starting, and there is no exigency calling for breaking, unlike the sick person: if he undertakes fasting then wants to break, it is permissible for him, and similarly if he falls ill after becoming a fasting person, it is permissible for him; because illness is a heavenly matter in which the servant has no choice. If the traveler broke his fast in the two mentioned images regarding the obligation of expiation: then expiation is not obligatory upon him; because it is only obligatory for breaking a obligatory fast without the presence of doubt. 'Does not waive' etc. due to the absence of travel that permits breaking which was a doubt in obligating expiation. When he falls ill after breaking the fast in a state of health, expiation is waived due to what we said that illness is a heavenly matter in which the servant has no choice, so it is as if he broke the fast while ill. When he finished the first section of acquired accidents, he began the second section, which is that which comes from another. He said: As for coercion: it is that a capable person compels another to something he does not want except out of fear of him, by threatening to inflict what he threatens, and it necessitates compulsion: i.e., the coerced person is compelled to do what he is ordered by what he fears for his life or a limb, such as the coercer saying: do this or I will kill you, or cut off your hand, then his choice is spoiled and his satisfaction is completely negated. The second type is coercion of compulsion: it is coercion by beating that does not endanger life, =
The images: the first image is that a traveler fasts then breaks his fast, the second: that a resident fasts then travels then breaks his fast, so no expiation in these two images. Yes, expiation is obligatory in the third image, that a resident fasts then breaks his fast before traveling. And it is

322
Types of Ikrah
with fatha (vowel)
And coercion in its entirety does not negate legal capacity, nor does it cause the removal of address in any case; because the coerced person is tested, i.e., in all the mentioned divisions, and testing realizes the address. Do you not see that he is wavering between obligation, prohibition, permissibility, and license. The coerced person sins in doing what he is coerced to do in one instance and is rewarded in another. So there is no license in killing and wounding for the coerced person
= or with a fetter, or imprisonment for a prolonged period, so in this type choice remains but he does not consent to what he does. And a third division remains which is that which does not negate satisfaction and does not spoil choice, such as threatening to imprison his father, son, wife, or the like. In this division, both satisfaction and choice remain. Legal capacity: neither capacity of obligation nor capacity of performance.

·
•
That
Removal of address: meaning its dropping from the coerced person. In any case, whether coercion is compelling or not, due to the existence of responsibility and intellect upon which address revolves. He is tested in the state of coercion just as he is tested in the state of choice. It realizes the address: because it is not established without it. Then he proved the existence of testing and realization of address by it: Obligation: as when one is coerced to eat carrion by death, then at that time it is obligatory upon him to eat it, and if he endures until death he sins; because eating it was permissible for him, as Allah Most High said: "Except what you are compelled to" (Al-An'am 6:119), thus permissibility was established by the exception, and one coerced to a permissible thing is obligated to do it. Prohibition: i.e., prohibited in the state of coercion as well, as when one is coerced to kill a Muslim unjustly, then if he endures until death he is rewarded. Permissibility: as when coerced to break the fast in Ramadan, then it becomes permissible for him to break. License: as when coerced to utter a word of disbelief, then at that time it is licensed for him, provided that belief is in his heart. The difference between license and permissibility is that in permissibility the prohibition is removed, while in license the prohibition is not removed but only the sin is removed. It is better not to mention permissibility; because if it is with sin in endurance then it is obligation, otherwise it is license. So the result is that it falls under obligation or license. 'He sins' therein: i.e., in proceeding with what he is coerced to do if it is forbidden, like killing a Muslim unjustly. 'Rewarded in another': in proceeding with what he is coerced to do if it is obligatory, like eating carrion when coerced by death. Both reward and sin only occur after the establishment of address. So it is established that the coerced person is addressed and tested. Then he pointed to examples of obligation, prohibition, license, and permissibility by his statement: 'So no license' etc. Regarding killing: i.e., killing another when he fears destruction of himself; because both are equal in deserving inviolability, so he cannot preserve his life by destroying another, so coercion becomes in the ruling of non-existence due to conflict of two sanctities without preponderance. Wounding: likewise, there is no license for him to wound another if told: cut off the hand of so-and-so or I will kill you, so if he cuts, he is sinful; because the limbs of a believer are in sanctity like the sanctity of his own self regarding others, so conflict occurs between the sanctity of himself and the sanctity of wounding another, so neither prevails =
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the types of coercion (ikrah) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author first explains the distinction between travel and illness regarding fasting: travel is a matter of choice, so once one starts fasting, he cannot break it without necessity, unlike illness which is involuntary. Then he introduces ikrah as an acquired accident (arid maktasab) from another person. Coercion is of two types: complete (kamil) which spoils choice and compels action through threat of death or severe harm, and deficient (qasir) which negates consent but does not compel. A third type is mentioned where neither choice nor consent is negated (e.g., threat to a relative). The key ruling is that coercion in all its forms does not remove legal capacity (ahliyyah) because the coerced person is still tested (mubtala) and addressed (mukhatab) by the Lawgiver. The commentary then gives examples: if coerced to eat carrion by death threat, it becomes obligatory (fard) to eat; if coerced to kill a Muslim unjustly, it remains prohibited (haram) and one should rather endure death; if coerced to break Ramadan fast, it becomes permissible (mubah); if coerced to utter disbelief, it is a license (rukhsa) provided the heart is firm in faith. Importantly, there is no license to kill or wound another even under coercion because the inviolability of the victim is equal to one's own, so the coercion is considered non-existent due to conflicting sanctities.

Key Terms
إكراه كامل (ikrah kamil): Complete coercion that spoils choice and necessitates compulsion, typically through threat of death or severe bodily harm.
إكراه قاصر (ikrah qasir): Deficient coercion that negates consent but does not eliminate choice; the coerced person can still choose but does not agree.
إلجاء (ilja'): Compulsion; the state where the coerced person has no realistic alternative but to comply.
أهلية (ahliyyah): Legal capacity to have rights and obligations, and to perform legal acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a person coerced to kill a Muslim by threat of death?
A) It is permissible for him to kill to save his own life.
B) It is obligatory for him to kill because his life is threatened.
C) There is no license for him to kill; he should rather endure death and be rewarded.
D) It is a license (rukhsa) for him to kill to avoid greater harm.

Key Takeaway
Coercion affects the legal classification of acts but does not remove the coerced person's status as a legal subject.
